STATEMENT OF PURPOSE
Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a servant of
purpose, and aesthetics took a backseat to utility. It wasn’t until I wandered through the vibrant streets of
Barcelona, on a trip during high school, that I discovered the quiet poetry of aesthetics in public spaces.
As I took on design assignments during undergraduate studies, I discovered it also had the power to effect social
change. What reinforced this was my on-site graduation project in collaboration with Grant Thornton, where I
worked with the indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive the endangered practice of crafting a Japi.
Immersing myself in the local culture deepened my understanding of community-driven design and its impact
on artisans and customers alike. From redesigning construction barricades and creating an educational activity
kit to teaching children about personal boundaries to developing a mechanical tool for flower vendors, my
bachelor’s in Industrial Design has reiterated the interconnection of design and community. This understanding
has been further strengthened through my work at Thinkonomers (HDL), where I learned to apply user feedback
in the design process; Pichkaari Design Studio, where I worked on rebranding projects and developed user flow
enhancement prototypes; Grant Thornton; and finally, in my current role as a UX/UI designer at BRND Studio,
where I handle end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards, educational platforms, and teaching tools. These
experiences have amalgamated into my design philosophy, based on creating superlative experiences for both
virtual and real spaces, and coalesced into my chosen career path in Information Experience Design.
After graduate studies, I plan to work as a UX Researcher or Innovation Strategist with creative firms like
Google or Huge Inc. on projects that blend strategy, design, and technology. My long-term vision is to become
an expert in human-centric design by creating versatile design solutions that have high utility, speak to the
audience, and help achieve the underlying business objectives. Inspired by my Japi Art project, I aim to work on
service ecosystems that address socio-economic inequities by co-creating accessible services like affordable
healthcare frameworks or skill-building digital platforms for marginalized communities and rural artisans.
The MS Information Experience Design program at Pratt is the perfect conduit for this. I am particularly
interested in the Practical Ethnography for User Experience, Speculative Design, and Information Architecture,
which encourage design thinking and conversance with industry-relevant tools. Pratt’s emphasis on experiential
learning through industry collaborations and live projects, and its state-of-the-art Semantic Lab and Observation
Room, provide an ideal environment for me to try my hand at emerging technologies like usability machines. I
am excited to collaborate with the intellectually spirited student cohort for the Pratt Design Challenge and
engage in team-based innovation to devise solutions for contemporary social and environmental challenges. By
making full use of these and the numerous other opportunities on campus, I do not doubt that I will become an
industry-ready professional, possessing the skills I need to achieve my goals.


--- Page 35 ---
RCA - Video Task script
We ask that you upload a two-minute video recorded on your phone or laptop, speaking to us
directly. High production qualities are not needed. We will review the work in your portfolio, so keep
your video simple.
In your video, please explain your motivation to be a service designer and provide an example of
your favourite service experience. Please then describe an aspect of Service Design you are most
passionate about. Finally, briefly describe your favourite project in your portfolio and why you chose
it.
This will be a chance to showcase your understanding of Service Design, your passion for studying at
a Master’s level and your ability to communicate the value and impact of your work.​
​
Points to cover -
-​

​
I’m Rachit Singhi, an industrial designer with a passion for creating thoughtful, impactful design
experiences and solutions.

Growing up in Bangalore, I was surrounded by residential and corporate complexes with more focus
on utility than aesthetics.  It wasn’t until I visited Barcelona, for a high school trip, that I discovered
the quiet poetry of aesthetics in public spaces. As I took on co-curricular design assignments during
undergraduate studies, I discovered art also had the power to effect social change. One project that
truly reinforced this was my on-site research with Grant Thornton, where I worked with indigenous
bamboo artisans from Assam to revive the endangered Japi craft. Through this and several other
community and people-oriented projects I have understood that design has a unique ability to
identify problems, craft solutions, and create smooth, user-centred experiences.

But this process requires deep research and analysis of the user’s ecosystem, along with careful
attention to where the most impactful changes can happen. I especially noted this during a unique
project I undertook - “The Life-Saving Dot,” also known as Jeevan Bindi - a decorative vermillion mark
adorned on the forehead by Indian women. My project blended culture, health, and technology, by
integrating salt into the traditional bindi and seeking to naturally remedy the iodine deficiency
problem in rural India. Similarly, my research into STI management practices among female sex
workers in Bhopal highlighted the intersection of design, public health, and social justice, giving a
voice to marginalized communities decidedly overlooked in policymaking.



--- Page 36 ---
What I loved most about all these projects and about service design as a field at large, is that I get to
work across disciplines, ideate like a tree and branch out through research and analysis that
oftentimes leads to novel and innovative outcomes.
My motivation to be a service designer stems from this ability to blend empathy, creativity, and
systemic thinking. I look forward to contributing to and learning from RCA's dynamic community,
where I hope to refine my skills and create solutions that matter.

STATEMENT OF PURPOSE

Growing up in Bangalore, I was surrounded by structures focused more on utility than aesthetics. However, my
travels to heritage sites in India introduced me to the profound impact of visual and experiential design. This
realisation deepened through my undergraduate studies in Industrial Design, where I explored how design can
drive social change. My work with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam reinforced my belief that design
doesn’t just change environments—it transforms lives, as I aimed to revive the endangered craft of Japi.
Integrating user research and analyzing ecosystems taught me how design interventions can create meaningful,
lasting impacts. During this time, I have explored the design process in areas like innovative product
development, user-centric methodologies, and systems thinking. This ability to use scientific advancements to
create new products and services that drive business and economy and, at the same time, make people’s lives
easier and more comfortable is something that excites and motivates me to build a long-term career in this field.
I now wish to augment my portfolio and train my design thinking mindset to scope, refine, and deliver
practicable solutions and translate abstract ideas, theories, and plans into practice through an MS in Information
Experience Design.
From a bird’s-eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility, and
sustainability. My academic and professional journey has been shaped by diverse experiences across industries
and disciplines. From redesigning construction barricades and creating educational activity kits that explore the
impact of touch on self-identity (paper selection in ICoRD – International Conference on Research into Design)
to developing mechanical tools for flower vendors, my projects have consistently emphasised the intersection of
design, usability, and community impact. My work at Thinkonomers (HDL) strengthened my ability to integrate
user feedback into the design process, while my role at Pichkaari Design Studio exposed me to branding and
user flow enhancements. At Grant Thornton, I worked on service-orientated design strategies, and my current
role as a UX/UI designer at BRND Studio involves handling end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards,
educational platforms, and teaching tools. These experiences have shaped my design philosophy, emphasizing
human-centered solutions.
Information Experience Design, for me, is a powerful tool to improve ineffective processes, streamline complex
systems, and enhance accessibility, especially in the context of India. My work with female sex workers in
Bhopal highlighted the need for better service ecosystems, as I observed how complicated government
procedures and inaccessible infrastructure create everyday hardships. Whether by revamping physical spaces or
designing user-friendly digital interfaces, I believe design can make essential services more approachable and
inclusive. Similarly, in business contexts, I have observed that reimagining workflows can effectively reduce
employee burnout and disengagement, highlighting the importance of customised, research-driven solutions
over generic approaches.
Sustainability is another area where Information Experience Design holds immense potential. Rapid economic
expansion in developing countries often comes at an environmental cost, but design can bridge the gap between
progress and sustainability. Whether in waste management, urban planning, or responsible consumption,
innovative service design solutions can help us grow without sacrificing the planet’s well-being.
Post-graduation, I aspire to work as an Experience Designer, UX Researcher, or Innovation Strategist where I
can follow, build, and reiterate my design philosophies and principles to drive impactful solutions. In the long
term, I aim to specialize in human-centric design, creating inclusive, high-utility frameworks that address
socio-economic disparities. For example, inspired by my work in Assam, I envision designing service
ecosystems such as skill-building digital platforms for rural artisans or accessible healthcare frameworks for
marginalized communities.

Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a servant
of purpose, and aesthetics took a backseat to utility. It wasn’t until I wandered through the vibrant streets
of Barcelona, on a trip during high school, that I discovered the quiet poetry of aesthetics in public spaces.
This revelation stirred a deep admiration within me during those early years and led me to pursue a degree
in design for my undergraduate education.
As I took on design assignments in college, I increasingly saw that it also had the unique power to effect
social change. What reinforced this was my on-site graduation project in collaboration with Grant

practice of crafting a Japi. Immersing myself in the local culture deepened my understanding of community-
driven design and its impact on the artisan and the other stakeholders associated with it. From developing
a mechanical tool for garland weaving that improved the efficiency and comfort of flower vendors to
creating an educational activity kit exploring the impact of a bad touch on self-identity for children, (the
latter is selected for presentation and publication at the International Conference on Research into Design
- ICoRD’25), all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, community, and sustainability.
This has coalesced into my chosen career path in Service Design. After graduate studies, I plan to work as
a Service Designer or Innovation Strategist with creative firms like IDEO, Fjord, or Deloitte Digital, on
projects that blend strategy, design, and technology. My long-term vision is to become an expert in looking
at design not just from a human-centric approach but a holistic systems-thinking perspective that keeps in
mind the ecosystem and sub-players around a user by creating versatile design solutions that have high
utility, speak to the audience, and help achieve the underlying business objectives.
The MA Service Design program at RCA is the perfect conduit to materialize these. I am particularly
interested in the Service Design Practice and Service Design in Context modules, which encourage
interdisciplinary design thinking and conversance with industry-relevant tools. RCA’s emphasis on practical
learning through industry collaborations, and live projects, and its state-of-the-art Computing &
Technology (CAT) Zones provide an ideal environment for me to experiment with emerging technologies. I
am excited to collaborate with the intellectually spirited student cohort for the AcrossRCA modules and
engage in team-based innovation to devise solutions for contemporary social and environmental
challenges. By making full use of these and the numerous other opportunities on campus, I do not doubt
that I will become an industry-ready professional, possessing the skills I need to achieve my goals.

-
Motivation to be a service designer and an example of my favourite service example
-

Script
I’m Rachit Singhi, an industrial designer with a passion for creating thoughtful, impactful design experiences
and solutions. During my time in college, as I worked on various design projects, I realized how design can
drive societal change. It became clear to me that design has a unique ability to identify problems, craft
solutions, and create smooth, user-centered experiences. One of my internships helped me understand just
how important it is to identify pain points, understand the environment around the user, and then develop
effective solutions. This process requires deep research and analysis of the user’s ecosystem, along with
careful attention to where the most impactful changes can happen. These experiences helped shape my design
philosophy, which eventually led me to focus on service design.

Service design excites me because it blends creativity, empathy, and strategy to address both tangible and
intangible challenges. What drives me is the chance to solve real-world problems through human-centered
innovation. A great example of this is “The Life-Saving Dot,” also known as Jeevan Bindi. This is a decorative
mark worn on the forehead by Indian women, especially Hindus. The project addresses iodine deficiency in
rural India by embedding iodine into the traditional bindi. It blends culture, health, and technology, creating a
solution that fits seamlessly into daily life. It removes barriers like taking pills or frequent doctor visits. This
project demonstrates key principles of service design, like working across disciplines, changing behavior
through culturally sensitive design, and creating scalable, sustainable solutions. Its simplicity and impact show
how powerful it is to design with empathy and consider the context.

One thing that excites me about service design is the uncertainty that persists throughout the process. The
design doesn’t start with a clear direction. Instead, research and analysis lead to an opportunity area for
intervention, which can be very multidisciplinary and sometimes totally different from what we expected.

A project that brought all of these ideas together for me was the Analysis of STI Management Practices,
Awareness Levels, and Access Barriers among Female Sex Workers in Bhopal. This project stands out because it
combined rigorous research with meaningful social impact. It allowed me to collaborate with a variety of
stakeholders to find the best solution. I chose this project because it focused on a critical public health issue
and gave a voice to a marginalized group, often overlooked in policymaking. It also taught me a lot about
addressing difficult social challenges.

Why do you want to attend SCAD? * (250 words max)

Over the past five years, my learnings have coalesced into my chosen career path in Service design.
After graduate studies, I plan to work as a Service Designer or Innovation Strategist with creative
firms like Deloitte Digital, or Fjords, on projects that blend strategy, design, and technology.
Specifically, I aim to work on service ecosystems that address pressing socio-economic inequities by
co-creating accessible services for marginalized communities - such as affordable healthcare
frameworks or skill-building digital platforms for rural artisans, inspired by my earlier work in Assam.
I am also open to working on digital products like fintech solutions, or interactive educational
platforms.  My long-term vision is to become an expert in human-centric design by creating versatile
design solutions that have high utility, speak to the audience, and help achieve the underlying
business objectives.
The MA in service design program at SCAD is the perfect conduit to materialize these. I am particularly
interested in the Mixed Methods Research, Visualizing Services, and Systemic Innovation for Service
Evolution modules which encourage design thinking and conversance with industry relevant tools.
SCAD’s emphasis on practical learning through industry collaborations, live projects, and its state-of-
the-art Gulfstream Center, model shop, and Computer labs provide an ideal environment for me to
experiment with emerging technologies such as AR/VR and Immersive media. I am excited to
collaborate with the intellectually spirited student cohort for the SCAD Challenge and engage in team-
based innovation to devise solutions for contemporary social and environmental challenges. By
making full use of these and the numerous other opportunities on campus, I have no doubt that I will
become an industry-ready professional, possessing the skills I need to achieve my goals.
Who is your favorite artist/designer and why? 250 word max
Amongst pioneers of human-centered service design, the person who inspires me most is Steve Krug.
His seminal work, Don’t Make Me Think, a book on web usability, has profoundly shaped my
understanding of designing for seamless experiences. Krug’s philosophy is radical in its simplicity:
services should be intuitive, reducing friction rather than adding complexity. I take from this that good
service design should focus on clarity, ensuring that interactions—both digital and physical—are as
effortless as possible. Krug emphasizes usability principles such as clear hierarchies and intuitive
pathways, which I have adapted to my work by prioritizing service flows that are frictionless, efficient,
and aligned with user needs.
Like many who start in the field, I initially focused on individual touchpoints, attempting to refine
specific interactions without fully considering the broader service ecosystem. Over time, through
practice, feedback, and learning from my missteps, I realized that impactful service design requires a
holistic approach—understanding user journeys, eliminating inefficiencies, and designing systems that
serve users in a way that feels natural and unobtrusive. This shift in perspective aligns with Krug’s
advocacy for removing unnecessary barriers and ensuring that services work so well that they become
almost invisible to the user.


--- Page 43 ---
I was fortunate to find a mentor who shares these values—Mr. Sudhir Bhatia, the founder of BRND,
my current studio. His approach to service design emphasizes that a well-designed system functions
like a well-oiled machine; it may not always be the most visually striking from the outside, but it
operates seamlessly, ensuring that every interaction contributes to a cohesive and effective
experience. Working with him has reinforced my belief that service design is not just about optimizing
interfaces but about orchestrating entire systems to serve people better.
While I do not have an absolute favorite influence, the teachings of Krug and the mentorship of Bhatia
have profoundly shaped my design ethos. By studying their work and integrating their methodologies,
I strive to create services that prioritize ease of use, reduce cognitive load, and ultimately enhance the
lives of those who interact with them.
How will your life and/or professional experience impact your ability to succeed
at SCAD? 250 word max
My Bachelor’s degree from India’s premier design school National Institute of Design (NID), has given
my design thought, creativity and critical thinking a decisively humane and people-oriented direction.
It introduced me to three facets of design: systems design, service design, and interaction design –
each with its unique applications that called for deeper exploration. The systems approach calls for
looking at a problem area holistically before identifying an intervention type, service design relies on
solving design problems with the aid of techno-digital interventions, whereas interaction designs shed
light on making such designs more user-centric.
As I took on design assignments in college, I increasingly saw that it also had the unique power to
effect social change. What reinforced this was my on-site graduation project in collaboration with
Grant Thornton where I worked with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive endangered
Japi crafts. Immersing myself in the local culture deepened my understanding of community-driven
design and the impact it can have on the artisan as well as a customer. In another project, I redesigned
construction barricades to improve safety for pedestrians during peak traffic hours. I used behavioral
mapping to understand challenges around their installation, inconveniences faced by Rickshaw and
Two-Wheeler drivers navigating around them, or pedestrians crossing near construction zones. After
a few rounds of on-ground testing of the full-scale prototype, I succeeded in designing a lightweight,
highly visible barricade that was easy to install, maintain, and store.
From developing a mechanical tool for garland weaving that improved the efficiency and comfort of
flower vendors to creating an educational activity kit exploring self-identity for children (the latter is
selected for presentation and publication at the International Conference on Research into Design -
ICoRD’25), all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, community, and
sustainability.
From a bird’s eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility,
and sustainability. This understanding has been further strengthened through my professional
experiences - at Thinkonomers (HDL) where I learned about the importance of user feedback in the
design process, Pichkaari Design Studio, where I worked on rebranding projects and developed user
flow enhancement prototypes, Grant Thornton, and finally in my current role as a UX/UI designer at
BRND Studio, where I handle end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards, educational platforms, and


--- Page 44 ---
teaching tools. These experiences have ingrained in me that user experience should be the top priority
in the design process and helped shape my own design philosophy, which is based on creating
superlative, easy to use experiences for modern spaces, both virtual and real. For example, in my initial
projects, I dove headfirst into design but with experience and trial and error, I realised that logic in
design will hold little value unless it is thoughtfully integrated with an intuitive understanding of how
it will be used by humans.

Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a
servant of purpose, and aesthetics took a backseat to utility. It wasn’t until I wandered through the
vibrant streets of Barcelona, for a trip during high school, that I discovered the quiet poetry of


--- Page 45 ---
aesthetics in public spaces. This revelation stirred a deep admiration within me during those early
years. My ensuing creative practice has been shaped by my undergraduate studies in Industrial Design
and diverse professional experiences. In this time, I have explored the design process in areas like
innovative product development, user-centric methodologies, and systems thinking. This ability to use
scientific advancements to create new products and services that drive business and economy and at
the same time make people’s lives easier and more comfortable is something that excites and
motivates me to build a long-term career in this field. I now wish to augment my portfolio and train
my design thinking mindset to scope, refine and deliver practicable solutions and translate abstract
ideas, theories and plans into practice through an MA inService Design.
This academic goal is a well-thought-out progression of my interests and skillset. I have always loved
art and craft as a young boy and knew that I wanted to work in a field that would allow me to express
my creativity and challenge me intellectually. My Bachelor’s degree from India’s premier design school
National Institute of Design (NID), has given my design thought, creativity and critical thinking a
decisively humane and people-oriented direction.  It introduced me to three facets of design: systems
design, service design, and interaction design – each with its unique applications that called for deeper
exploration. The systems approach calls for looking at a problem area holistically before identifying an
intervention type, service design relies on solving design problems with the aid of techno-digital
interventions, whereas interaction designs shed light on making such designs more user-centric.
As I took on design assignments in college, I increasingly saw that it also had the unique power to
effect social change. What reinforced this was my on-site graduation project in collaboration with
Grant Thornton where I worked with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive endangered
Japi crafts. Immersing myself in the local culture deepened my understanding of community-driven
design and the impact it can have on the artisan as well as a customer. In another project, I redesigned
construction barricades to improve safety for pedestrians during peak traffic hours. I used behavioral
mapping to understand challenges around their installation, inconveniences faced by Rickshaw and
Two-Wheeler drivers navigating around them, or pedestrians crossing near construction zones. After
a few rounds of on-ground testing of the full-scale prototype, I succeeded in designing a lightweight,
highly visible barricade that was easy to install, maintain, and store.
In another creative project, I designed a mechanical tool for garland weaving that improved the
efficiency and comfort of flower vendors. My hometown Bengaluru, and my study domicile Madhya
Pradesh, are both renowned for their temple heritage where millions of devotees offer flowers for
worship, in turn leading to a booming flower industry. Observing the beautiful garland creations of
the vendors I also noticed many of them faced repetitive strain injuries due to manual weaving
techniques. Using a participatory design approach, I co-created an ingenious tool that at once
remedied the physical duress of the weaving and enhanced the speed and productivity of the garland
weavers.
Prior to this, I worked on two tech-intensive projects. The first was an analysis of STI management
practices, awareness levels, and barriers restricting access to services among female sex workers
(FSWs) in Bhopal. Employing the snowball sampling technique, I was able to locate their work zones.
But this was only the beginning - the real challenge was gaining their trust and convincing them of the
larger social purpose of my work. The research-heavy nature of the project further complicated the
process but using methods like GIGA mapping user journeys, and iceberg models, we developed a


--- Page 46 ---
customized Trigger and Response Model to comprehend the data and interpret findings more
effectively. The resultant research provided recommendations for community-centric support
systems, such as mobile clinics and awareness programs. In the second project, I created an
educational activity kit exploring self-identity for children. Using techniques like user journey mapping,
participatory testing, and iterative prototyping, I built a fun tool to help children understand and
express their feelings. My research paper on the same selected for presentation and publication at
the International Conference on Research into Design - ICoRD’25.
From a bird’s eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility,
and sustainability. This understanding has been further strengthened through my professional
experiences - at Thinkonomers (HDL) where I learned about the importance of user feedback in the
design process, Pichkaari Design Studio, where I worked on rebranding projects and developed user
flow enhancement prototypes, Grant Thornton, and finally in my current role as a UX/UI designer at
BRND Studio, where I handle end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards, educational platforms, and
teaching tools. These experiences have ingrained in me that user experience should be the top priority
in the design process and helped shape my own design philosophy, which is based on creating
superlative, easy to use experiences for modern spaces, both virtual and real. For example, in my initial
projects, I dove headfirst into design but with experience and trial and error, I realised that logic in
design will hold little value unless it is thoughtfully integrated with an intuitive understanding of how
it will be used by humans.
Collectively, these five years of formative learnings have coalesced into my chosen career path in
Service Design. After graduate studies, I plan to work as a Service Designer or Innovation Strategist
with creative firms like Deloitte Digital, Fjord, or IDEO, on projects that blend strategy, design, and
technology. Specifically, I aim to work on service ecosystems that address pressing socio-economic
inequities by co-creating accessible services for marginalized communities—such as affordable
healthcare frameworks or skill-building digital platforms for rural artisans, inspired by my earlier work
in Assam. I am also open to working on digital products like fintech solutions or interactive educational
platforms. My long-term vision is to become an expert in human-centric design by creating versatile
design solutions that have high utility, speak to the audience, and help achieve the underlying business
objectives.
The MA in Service Design program at SCAD is the perfect conduit to materialize these. I am particularly
interested in the Mixed Methods Research, Visualizing Services, and Systemic Innovation for Service
Evolution modules, which encourage design thinking and conversance with industry-relevant tools.
SCAD’s emphasis on practical learning through industry collaborations, live projects, and its state-of-
the-art Gulfstream Center, model shop, and computer labs provide an ideal environment for me to
experiment with emerging technologies such as AR/VR and immersive media. I am excited to
collaborate with the intellectually spirited student cohort for the SCAD Challenge and engage in team-
based innovation to devise solutions for contemporary social and environmental challenges. By
making full use of these and the numerous other opportunities on campus, I have no doubt that I will
become an industry-ready professional, possessing the skills I need to achieve my goals.

Hi, I am Rachit Singhi. A user-centered industrial designer who is extremely passionate about creating
impactful and thoughtful design experiences and solutions. With the same motive in mind, I had the
opportunity to work as a summer trainee in Grant Thornton Bharat, which is an organization that
primarily provides services to the business sectors like auditing and accounting.

One such service that the organization also provides is by collaborating with multiple rural stakeholders
as part of their corporate social responsibility program. This is where I had the opportunity as a designer
to work with the organization and collaborate with the bamboo artisans of Assam, which is an
economically backward state in India, for a duration of six months. I spent my time in the cluster
understanding the crafting techniques used by the artisans to make a Japi, which is a traditional bamboo
hat worn by the people of Assam, and developed two product ranges which were derived from these
already-known crafting techniques by the artisans but had a higher market appeal, and as a result, could
increase the revenue generation and income for these artisans. Although I was very quick to realize the
loopholes that existed that existed in the entire project in the first month itself.

Considering I wasn’t the first to conduct such training with the artisans, it raised an important question:
Why had the previous training not been effective? This question opened up a can of worms, allowing
me to examine the entire project a lot more holistically. I began questioning whether simply having an
output that the organization thinks is impactful was enough or was it far more important to consider
how to deliver that output to maximize its impact, considering what the key stakeholders are looking
for from such interventions and training given to them.

And hence, this project became a lens through which I understood the importance of service design. It
highlighted the importance of not developing a solution for the identified problem area until proper
research has been undertaken with all of the players involved in the ecosystem. Instead of taking a top-
down approach to training deployment, I took a bottom-up approach to training needs assessment.
With this approach in mind, I reassessed the designs of product development that were being taught to
these artisans during these training sessions and instead developed designs that didn’t feel as alien to
them
and
had
a
sense
of
belongingness
to
the
Japi.

This project highlighted the idea that having a solution isn’t enough; but rather, how the intervention is
delivered can make all the difference in achieving a meaningful impact that actually sticks and can be
applied to a real-world scenario.

-
The core of industrial design is solving problems, and how one solves that problem is
driven by research and interacting with people. Industrial design is a multidisciplinary
approach that can involve creating digital/tangible products, working with materials, or
solving problems through strategies like systems design.
-
Since childhood, I’ve been drawn to working with materials, whether it’s a single piece of
cardboard or a scrap of cloth, all for personal fulfillment. At the same time I was
unknowingly drawn to the basics of digital design via school projects or extracurricular
activities like making a short film or designing a flyer, where we had  to convey a
message visually and effectively.  Lastly ,I’ve always been passionate about talking to
people. In school, I was part of the “National Service Scheme,” where we visited a
hospital for mentally challenged individuals, a first experience of such for me. There, I
discovered how much joy I find in conversing with people and hearing stories and
experiences that I might never encounter again.
-
After my first year, I was unsure about which specialization to choose. My faculty
suggested Industrial Design, which combines people interaction, product design, and
digital design—exactly the blend I was looking for.

(I am not exactly changing fields - but I am choosing a subset of industrial design)
1. Service design
What attracts me most to service design is that it shares similarities with industrial
design—it's flexible, solution-centric, and allows for entering a project with ambiguity,
letting research guide the end product. Additionally, it is highly multidisciplinary, fostering
cross-learning from various design practices.

My decision to pursue service design stems from a personal experience. During my six-
month graduation project, I was given a design brief from an organization to create two
product ranges from bamboo, a material already used by local craftsmen. The goal was
to introduce and train them in modern product designs that could sell better in the


--- Page 50 ---
market. A secondary deliverable was to train the wives of these artisans to expand their
involvement in the craft and teach them ways to improve marketability.

During my research, I realized that while the organization had clear goals and
deliverables, the real issue lay elsewhere. Previous training failed because they didn’t
focus on the cultural preservation of the craft or on empowering artisans to make
decisions. This gap between what artisans wanted and what organizations were
providing made me realize that companies need to consider not just what they deliver,
but how they deliver it for the greatest impact.
 (Not sure if this would go in the SOP or the essays)

2. Interaction Design/Information Experience Design
I was initially torn between Communication Design and Industrial Design.
Communication Design attracted me because it was the field I was first exposed to—
creating logos, designing layouts for soft boards in school, and making visually appealing
school projects. My limited knowledge pushed me towards Communication Design at
first. However, I soon realized that Industrial Design offered the perfect blend for me. It
allowed me to explore both physical and digital product development while combining
research and engaging with people, which I enjoy.

Over time, I discovered that Interaction Design isn’t just about making things look good.
It's about making them feel right and work intuitively, grounded in a deep understanding
of people’s needs.

Interaction Design lies at the intersection of technology, psychology, and design, enabling
you to solve problems, push boundaries, and create meaningful experiences—without
being restricted to one discipline.

Q2. In your most recent internship or experience (or your longest internship/experience), what

Guidelines to answer

-
The biggest learning I’ve had is that user experience comes first, followed by user
interface design. The experience a digital product should provide to the user must be
defined first before jumping into designing the product itself.
-
The rules that define what the user wants from the product and what will make it easiest
for them to use should be prioritized. For example, deciding which features should be
front and center or finding ways to reduce the time it takes for users to complete a task.
-
When I started my job, I would jump straight into designing a product after the first
interaction with the client. However, I later realized the importance of understanding the
requirements in depth, not just at a surface level. This is a habit many designers likely
have too.
-
The habit in the UI design industry is that "what looks pretty sells the fastest" was proven
wrong through experience. Additionally, this approach saves time, as understanding the


--- Page 51 ---
consumer’s requirements from the start leads to fewer revisions and a more direct path
to the right solution.
-
Hence, Users defined experience is the first step which is followed by Designing the
interface.

Q6. Key Academic Projects (max 3) including final year project (100 words maximum each)
Guidelines to answer
▪
Mention the name of the project and period that you’re referring to; Discuss the scope and
main challenge of the project
▪
Provide unique learnings that are relevant to your field
▪
Discuss tools/techniques/processes that were used or needed for the project

1. Apunar Japi – translates to "Our Japi" in Assamese (Final Year Project) – 6 months
The project aimed to collaborate with local artisans in Nalbari, Assam, skilled in
traditional bamboo weaving techniques, to create two interior product ranges with high
perceived value, helping artisans increase their income. Additionally, I trained over 25
women from the artisan village on how to improve the marketability of the products. The
main challenge was building trust with the villagers, especially since I didn’t speak their
language and looked different from them. I overcame this by spending hours each day
with the villagers, dressing like them, and learning their language. The key learning from
this project was how to adjust to new situations. Tools used included Kevin Lynch’s
mapping techniques, craft process documentation, data analysis, rapid prototyping, and
diverse ideation.

2. STI Management amongst Female Sex Workers of Bhopal – (Last Semester Project) – 8
weeks
This project analyzed STI management practices, awareness levels, and barriers
restricting access to services among female sex workers (FSWs) in Bhopal. It involved
examining the roles of various stakeholders and the effectiveness of local support
systems. The biggest challenge was identifying sex workers due to the secrecy
surrounding their profession. Using snowball sampling, we were able to locate them. The
second challenge was gaining their trust, as they feared authorities like the police. Given
the research-heavy nature of the project, we identified various pain points using methods
like GIGA mapping, user journeys, and iceberg models to understand the data.
Additionally, we invented our own data analysis and visualization model, the “Trigger and
Response Model,” to interpret findings more effectively.

3. Redesigning Construction Barricades – (Last Semester Project) – 6 weeks
The project aimed to redesign construction barricades to enhance public safety in busy
roads and areas. The goal was to make barricades more visible and safer for pedestrians
while improving factors like ease of installation, maintenance, and storage for other
stakeholders. This would ultimately increase the barricades' overall safety, security, and
longevity. We conducted behavioral mapping and studied various stakeholders, including
workers who install the barricades, as well as drivers of cars and rickshaws, to
understand their challenges and requirements. The primary challenge was addressing
the diverse group of stakeholders involved: those who build the barricades, direct users,
and numerous indirect users. Additionally, we created a full-scale prototype using actual
materials and tested it with key stakeholders, incorporating feedback for a second round
of revisions.

1. Research Paper - ‘International Conference on Research into Design’ (ICoRD’25) -
Research Paper Selection under Department of Design - IIT Hyderabad | 2025
(This paper is selected for the conference but has not been published yet.)
This study explores the psychological effects of touch on self-identity, focusing on
trauma and personality development. Through semi-structured interviews and visual
representations (n=15), we identified the influence of gender biases, societal norms, and
educational gaps in childhood responses to touch. Based on these findings, we designed
an educational activity kit for children (ages 8-13) to teach boundaries and emotional
responses, aiming to promote healthy conversations and early education about touch.
The kit included collaborative exercises, a board game, and other interactive elements.
The paper offers a unique understanding of “grey touches,” an under-explored topic, and
provides a direct intervention for an age group that is rarely addressed. This research
deepened my understanding of the importance of consolidating research and how a well-
constructed paper can lead to future developments and interventions in identified
problem areas.

During college, I participated in several stage programs and even anchored events.
However, theatre was a completely new way for me to engage with an audience. I
performed the lead role in front of 250-300 people during a fest, after going through
auditions. Being selected boosted my confidence. Additionally, I contributed creatively by
helping design the set. This experience taught me the value of trying new things and
trusting my instincts when faced with challenges.

STATEMENT OF INTENT
​
Amongst stalwarts of user-centered design, the person who inspires me most is Steve Krug. His
seminal work, Don’t Make Me Think, a book on web usability, has profoundly shaped my
understanding of design. Krug is radical, emphasizing function over aesthetics and advocating for
self-evident designs that prioritize user experience above all else. What I read this as is that good
design should simplify, not complicate. Krug does this by focusing on clear visual hierarchies and
ensuring navigation serves its purpose. I have adapted this idea to my work, believing that a product’s
experience holds more value and precedence over the visual interface. Like all novice designers, I too
initially focused on surface-level requirements, jumping straight into creating the best aesthetic
designs possible. But over time, through practice and feedback (and some humbling mistakes!), I
realized the importance of going to the root of user needs, reducing fluff, and making designs that
embody clarity—an approach Krug champions.
I was searching for like-minded professionals who shared this philosophy and have been fortunate to
work with Mr. Sudhir Bhatia, the founder of BRND—my current studio. His emphasis on
experience-driven design has reiterated that a design is like a well-oiled machine; although at times
unpleasant to the eye from the outside, it works as smoothly as butter on the inside, fulfilling its
intrinsic purpose. Working with him has allowed me to stay true to these principles, reaffirming that
thoughtful, user-centered design is at the heart of innovation.
While I do not have an absolute favorite, Krug’s teachings and Bhatia’s mentorship have made a
significant impact on my personal values and design ethos. By studying their work and integrating
their techniques into my design process, I hope to create functional and intuitive products and
services.
I envision the future designer Rachit, working on ideas that blend strategy, design, and technology,
becoming an expert in human-centric design by creating versatile design solutions that have high
utility, speak to the audience, and help achieve the underlying business objectives. My collaboration
with bamboo artisans in Assam, to help promote the endangered Japi craft, has shown me how design
can breathe new life into traditional art forms. In the future, I see myself creating modular,
self-sustaining products and services like furniture with IoT sensors to monitor air quality or
crosswalks that adjust automatically to ensure the safety of pedestrians during peak hours. Although
this may sound utopian, with the right mix of practicality and technology, it can become a blueprint
for a better future.
In preparation for such a lofty career progression, I have earned a bachelor’s degree from India’s
premier design school, the National Institute of Design (NID).  I have worked on innovative projects
like redesigning construction barricades, creating an educational activity kit to teach children about
personal boundaries, developing a mechanical tool for flower vendors, and promoting a dying
indigenous bamboo art form from Assam. I have also challenged myself by undertaking industry
internships to learn the real-world applicability of my studies. At Thinkonomers (HDL), I learned to
apply user feedback in the design process; my work with Pichkaari Design Studio revolved around
rebranding projects and developing user flow enhancement prototypes; at Grant Thornton, I undertook
a community-driven design project to revive endangered Japi craft of Assam; and finally, in my
current role as a UX/UI designer at BRND Studio, I handle end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards,
educational platforms, and teaching tools.


--- Page 56 ---
In addition to my academic and professional pursuits, I have actively participated in various
extracurricular activities that have refined my teamwork and leadership and given me a well-rounded
skill set that I believe will enable me to perform at my best during my master's.
It has been an incredible journey these past few years, and I am now excited to savor every experience
of graduate life, whether it is the rigorous course curriculum, access to the SVA alumni network, or
the prospect of living in NYC. As an independent individual, I would appreciate your feedback on my

STATEMENT OF PURPOSE - 01
​
How have your prior activities and experiences (e.g., things you have done at school, in the workplace, in
your community, and/or extracurricular activities) prepared you for this program and this field? Please
provide specific examples. [600 words maximum]
Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a servant of
purpose, and aesthetics took a backseat to utility. It wasn’t until I wandered through the vibrant streets of
Barcelona, for a trip during high school, that I discovered the quiet poetry of aesthetics in public spaces. This
revelation stirred a deep admiration within me during those early years. My ensuing creative practice has been
shaped by my undergraduate studies in Industrial Design and diverse professional experiences. In this time, I
have explored the design process in areas like innovative product development, user-centric methodologies, and
systems thinking. This ability to use scientific advancements to create new products and services that drive
business and the economy while making people’s lives easier and more comfortable excites and motivates me to
build a long-term career in this field.
I pursued my Bachelor’s degree from India’s premier design school, the National Institute of Design (NID),
where my design thinking and creativity took a decisively humane and people-oriented direction. During my
time at NID, I was introduced to three facets of design: systems design, service design, and interaction design.
The systems approach taught me to look at a problem area in its entirety before identifying potential
interventions, while interaction design focused on technological interventions. But service design appealed to
me the most due to its ability to solve problems through user-centricity and accessible designs.
In college, I took on multiple design assignments that further helped me identify my design niche. For my
graduation assignment with Grant Thornton, I worked with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive
endangered Japi crafts and saw firsthand how design had the unique power to effect social change. For a similar
project, by using a participatory design approach, I designed a mechanical tool that alleviated the physical strain
and increased the productivity of flower vendors engaged in garland-weaving activities. I also used behavioral
mapping to understand the challenges faced by pedestrians and drivers and designed a lightweight, highly
visible construction barricade to improve safety for pedestrians during peak traffic hours.
Beyond these community-based projects, I worked on two tech-intensive projects. In the first, I analyzed STI
management practices and barriers restricting access to services among female sex workers (FSWs) in Bhopal.
Employing snowball sampling techniques to locate their work zones and, using methods like GIGA mapping
user journeys, I developed a Trigger and Response Model to better interpret the data. The project led to
recommendations for community-centric support systems such as mobile clinics and awareness programs. In the
second project, I created an educational activity kit to help children explore self-identity. By utilizing user
journey mapping, participatory testing, and iterative prototyping, I built a tool to help children understand and
express their feelings, which was later presented and published at the International Conference on Research into
Design (ICoRD’25).
My myriad set of formative learnings has coalesced into my passion for making a career in human-centered
design and user experience research. I have come to appreciate that product design, albeit a creative process,
requires conversance with software design tools, user-centric methodologies, and an allied understanding of
socio-economic dynamics and business management to truly understand the consumer ask. While I have been
working on developing the aforementioned proficiencies, I require advanced training and international exposure
to the field, which I hope to gain through the MS Human Centered Design & Engineering program at the
University of Washington.

socio-economic inequities and improve overall quality of life.
By making full use of these and the numerous other opportunities on campus, I do not doubt that I will become

STATEMENT OF PURPOSE
​
Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a

productivity of flower vendors engaged in garland weaving activities. I also used behavioral mapping
to understand the challenges faced by pedestrians and drivers and designed a lightweight, highly
visible construction barricade to improve safety for pedestrians during peak traffic hours.
Beyond these community-based projects, I worked on two tech-intensive projects. In the first, I
analyzed STI management practices and barriers restricting access to services among female sex
workers (FSWs) in Bhopal. Employing snowball sampling techniques to locate their work zones and,
using methods like GIGA mapping user journeys, I developed a Trigger and Response Model to better
interpret the data. The project led to recommendations for community-centric support systems such as
mobile clinics and awareness programs. In the second project, I created an educational activity kit to
help children explore self-identity. By utilizing user journey mapping, participatory testing, and
iterative prototyping, I built a tool to help children understand and express their feelings, which was
later presented and published at the International Conference on Research into Design (ICoRD’25).
From a bird's-eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility,
and sustainability. This understanding has been further strengthened through my professional
experiences—at Thinkonomers (HDL), where I learned about the importance of user feedback in the
design process, at Pichkaari Design Studio, where I worked on rebranding projects and developed user
flow enhancement prototypes, Grant Thornton, and finally in my current role as a UX/UI designer at
BRND Studio, where I handle end-to-end design for SaaS dashboards, educational platforms, and
teaching tools. These experiences have ingrained in me that user experience should be the top priority
in the design process and helped shape my design philosophy, which is based on creating superlative,
easy-to-use experiences for modern spaces, both virtual and real. For example, in my initial projects, I
dove headfirst into design but with experience and trial and error, I realized that logic in design will
hold little value unless it is thoughtfully integrated with an intuitive understanding of how it will be
used by humans.
Collectively, these five years of formative learning have coalesced into my chosen career path in
Service Design. After graduate studies, I plan to work as a Service Designer or Innovation Strategist
with creative firms like Adobe or Deloitte, on projects that blend strategy, design, and technology. I
am passionate about working on service ecosystems that address critical challenges in accessibility,
equity, and inclusion for underserved populations.  For example, I want to design services for
marginalized communities, such as affordable healthcare frameworks or digital platforms for skilling
and business training for rural and tribal artisans. These goals are inspired by my earlier project in
Assam, where I worked with artisans to develop community-driven solutions. Additionally, I am open


--- Page 60 ---
to contributing to digital products like fintech solutions or interactive educational platforms, using my
design background to create user-friendly, impactful tools. My long-term vision is to become an
expert in human-centric design by creating versatile design solutions that have high utility, speak to
the audience, and help achieve the underlying business objectives.
I have come to appreciate that product design, albeit a creative process, requires conversance with
software design tools, user-centric methodologies, and an allied understanding of socio-economic
dynamics and business management to truly understand the consumer's question. While I have been
working on developing the aforementioned proficiencies, I require advanced training and international
exposure to the field, which I hope to gain through the MA in Service Design at SCAD.

Q1. Tell us about someone you look up to and why. The person can be a designer, a friend, a
relative, someone in your neighborhood—anyone who inspires you and your creative practice.

The person who inspires me is Steve Krug, the author of Don’t Make Me Think, a seminal book on
web usability that shaped my understanding of design. His principles emphasize function over
aesthetics, advocating for intuitive, self-evident designs that prioritize user experience above all else.
From creating clear visual hierarchies to ensuring navigation serves its purpose, Krug's approach
resonated deeply with my belief that a product’s experience must come first, followed by its
interface. Early in my career, I used to focus on surface-level requirements, jumping straight into
designs. Over time, I realized the value of digging deeper into user needs, reducing unnecessary
complexity, and designing with clarity—an approach Krug champions. I was searching for like-
minded professionals who shared this philosophy when I came across Sudhir Bhatia, the founder of
my current studio. His emphasis on experience-driven design reinforced my confidence that not
everything is about making things look pretty—it's about making them work. Working with him has
inspired me to stay true to these principles, reaffirming that thoughtful, user-centered design is the
foundation of impactful work.

Q2. Imagine it is 20 years from the present moment. Describe your life—what kind of work are you

interaction design, and how has the field of interaction design shaped your life?

As an interaction designer, I’m passionate about creating solutions that make a real difference,

work has always been about finding impactful problem areas and addressing them in a way that
empowers people and creates lasting change. Over the years, I’ve had the chance to work with
diverse groups, from female sex workers and transgender individuals to artisans and survivors of
abuse. These experiences have taught me how powerful design can be in bridging gaps and helping
people improve their lives.

Q1. What excites you about the field of interaction design at this moment in your life?

communities that have very little access to it because of a lack of knowledge on how to maximize its benefits.

interactions with services much simpler, easier, and quicker. Ultimately, interaction design is the process of
transforming challenges into opportunities by leveraging technology and design to create experiences that feel
natural, easy, and impactful for the user.

Script:

I am Rachit Singhi, a user-centered industrial designer passionate about creating thoughtful, impactful
design experiences and solutions. With the same motive in mind, I undertook a project as a summer
trainee with Grant Thornton Bharat, a corporation that provides services to business sectors such as
accounting and auditing.
This project gave me insight into an industry that I wouldn’t have conventionally considered, solely
because of the internship opportunity I had gotten. I had the chance to work in the organization as a
designer for my graduation project. I collaborated with the bamboo artisans of Assam for six months,
developing novel product ranges as part of their Corporate Social Responsibility Programme.
During this project, I realized the importance of service design in any industry and how the lack of the
right smart work could overshadow the results of hard work, and Grant Thornton, in my personal
opinion, did lack here. Grant Thornton is an Indian organization that has major collaborations with the
government to become the leading firm in the auditing and accounting sector from India, replacing
other multinational corporations whose base doesn’t stem from India to make the country self-reliant.
On interacting with a few employees of the organization, it was realized that the organization is hiring
employees who have worked in the Big 4 earlier, providing them with a better scale with the motive
of being able to compete with these challenging firms through experienced employees. However, this
limits the number of employees the organization can hire due to the increased pay to be given to them
and, as a response, increases the workload among these limited employees. Additionally, these
employees are experienced in dealing with large public sector entities and organizations like Fortune
500 companies, which have a well-defined structure of roles and responsibilities. On the other hand,
Grant Thornton is still a growing organization dealing with mid-sized businesses and government
entities, where there are well-defined departments, but the division of work isn’t balanced, hindering
the overall journey of a customer and user.
While the organization is making an effort, what Grant Thornton has not recognized is that the
competing organizations have a defined brand perception and market positioning with a global reach
and scale that have been developed and built over the years. Replicating a model from an already
established organization might not prove to be as effective, and identifying new and unique problem


--- Page 66 ---
areas novel to the organization would prove a lot more effective in its success and in employee and
client retention. Due to issues like these, initiatives like CSR, which can help in brand positioning in
the competitive market, also suffer majorly, with no proper framework in place for smooth
productivity to take place.

This experience deeply challenged my understanding of service design by revealing how crucial it is
to have a well-thought-out framework in place to ensure both employee well-being and customer
satisfaction. I saw firsthand how the lack of a balanced, user-centered approach can undermine even
the most promising initiatives, such as CSR, preventing them from reaching their full potential. Being
part of a growing organization like Grant Thornton allowed me to witness the struggles of adapting
service design principles in an environment still defining its identity and workflows. These challenges
pushed me to think more critically and creatively about how design can solve systemic inefficiencies.
It was this realization that inspired me to pursue the Service Design course, as I want to develop the
skills and knowledge to design solutions that drive meaningful and lasting impact.

Script:

I am Rachit Singhi, a user-centered industrial designer passionate about creating thoughtful, impactful
design experiences and solutions. With the same motive in mind, I undertook a project as a summer
trainee with Grant Thornton Bharat. This corporation provides services to business sectors, such as
accounting and auditing.

One such service the organization works on involves collaborating with multiple rural sector
stakeholders as part of its corporate social responsibility program. I had the opportunity to work at the
organization as a designer for my graduation project, where I collaborated with the bamboo artisans of
Assam for six months, understanding their craft techniques and developing two product ranges that
appealed to the current market, thereby increasing the income levels of the artisans.

While the project seemed impactful on the surface—given that the organization defined the required
output beforehand to create a greater good for the artisans—I quickly realized the number of
loopholes that existed in the pre-defined output. These issues indicated a lack of research and an
inadequate understanding of how the problem needed to be solved by meeting the requirements of the
artisans from the project.

I understand and realize that CSRs and rural development aren’t the primary goals of Grant Thornton
and hence, it isn’t the mistake of the organization for the loopholes to exist because of the limited
resources available for such initiatives to be taken to their full capacity. Still, the project helped me
have a personal realization of the importance of service design.

Considering I wasn’t the first to conduct such training with the artisans, it raised an important
question: Why had previous training sessions not been effective? This question opened up a can of
worms, allowing me to examine the entire project holistically. I began questioning whether simply
having an output that the organization thinks is impactful was enough or if it was far more important
to consider how to deliver the output to maximize its impact, considering what the key stakeholders
are looking for from such interventions and training.



--- Page 68 ---
This project became a lens through which I understood the importance of service design. It
highlighted the importance of not coming up with a solution for the identified problem area until
proper research has been undertaken with the primary players involved in the ecosystem. This project
highlighted the idea that just having a particular solution isn’t sufficient; rather, how the intervention
is delivered can make all the difference in achieving a meaningful impact that sticks and is applied in
a real-world scenario.

For instance, in this scenario, the training conducted in the past disconnected the artisans from their
culture, teaching them techniques that were unfamiliar and introducing products without adequately
explaining how to market these newer products through novel marketing channels. These drawbacks
and other similar issues highlight the importance of appropriate and multi-faceted solutions.

Script:

I am Rachit Singhi, a user-centered industrial designer passionate about creating thoughtful, impactful
design experiences and solutions. With the same motive in mind, I undertook a project as a summer
trainee with Grant Thornton Bharat, a corporation that provides services to business sectors, such as
accounting and auditing.

One such service the organization works on involves collaborating with multiple rural sector
stakeholders as part of its corporate social responsibility program. I had the opportunity to work at the
organization as a designer for my graduation project, where I collaborated with the bamboo artisans of
Assam for six months, understanding their craft techniques and developing two product ranges that
appealed to the current market, thereby increasing the income levels of the artisans.

While the project seemed impactful on the surface—given that the required output was defined by the
organization beforehand to create a greater good for the artisans—I quickly realized the number of
loopholes that existed in the pre-defined output. These issues indicated a lack of research and an
inadequate understanding of how the problem needed to be solved by actually meeting the
requirements of the artisans from the project.

Considering I wasn’t the first to conduct such training with the artisans, it raised an important
question: Why had previous training sessions not been effective? This question opened up a can of
worms, allowing me to examine the entire project a lot more holistically. I began questioning whether
simply having an output that the organization thinks is impactful was enough or if it was far more
important to consider how to deliver the output to maximize its impact, keeping in mind what the key
stakeholders are looking for from such interventions and training.

This project became a lens through which I understood the importance of service design. The
importance of not coming up with a solution for the identified problem area until proper research has
been undertaken with the primary players involved in the ecosystem. This project highlighted the idea
that just having a particular solution isn’t sufficient; rather, the way the intervention is delivered can
make all the difference in achieving a meaningful impact that sticks and is applied in a real-world
scenario.

culture, teaching them techniques that were unfamiliar and introducing products without adequately

Hi, I am Rachit Singhi. I have pursued my bachelor's degree in industrial design from the
National Institute of Design. Currently I work as a UI/UX designer, that is, a user interface
and user experience designer. I believe my greatest strengths are my ability to empathize

FACT EVALUATION QUESTIONS: Aimed at understanding your interpersonal skills by

Q1. What are your greatest strengths and weaknesses?
I would like to highlight and talk about 3 of my strengths and 2 weaknesses
1. One of my strengths stem from my ability of being a process driven designer where I try to
logically answer questions throughout the process to move to the next step. This allows me
to identify loop holes in services and systems and improve on ways to deliver a solution. For
example while working with grant thornton, even though I was given a deifned deliverable
since the beginning I was able to better refine the quality of them by understanding the
requirments of the primary stakeholders from the deliverables which not done before.
2. This leads to cullminates into my second strength of identifying impactful opportunity
areas to solve a problem.
3. Lastly, I believe I do have certain leadership qualities while working in groups that allow
me to allocate and manage work that needs to be done in a pragmatic manner.

Weaknesses -
1. But this has also led to a discovery where I might seem a little bossy and pushy at time.
With design being very subjective according to everyones individual opinion, I end up being
puhsy of my opinions leading to microminaging the projects.
2. Lastly,  I struggle with time management and allocation of the right amount of time to the
different stages in a design process. Especially when it comes to knowing when to end the
research stage and begin ideating on the deliverables. For example while working on a
project around understanding accessibility barriers to STI infrastructures for female sex
workers in Bhopal, we led to some key opportuniy areas but because of the time crunch we
failed to develop a conrete solution.


--- Page 74 ---

Q2. What is your greatest achievement/major accomplishment?
Q3. What is your biggest failure or challenge? And learning from failure?
Achievement -
1. The greatest achievement of mine came extremely unexpectedly to me with me being
awarded as the gold medalist of my batch in college. While I understand marks are very
subjective and that this might not be the greatest achievement for many, It was for me
because it helped me in my personal growth a lot. With NID being an extremely competitive
college I was constantly in a position of self doubt, this affected my ability to communicate
my ideas which is primary for a designer. But this achievement overturned these doubts and
gave me a sense of confidence which I was earlier lacking making me believe a lot more on
my abilities.

Failiure -
1. During my second year of college, I set a goal to join a prestigious service-oriented
organization I deeply admired. After successfully clearing the competitive portfolio round, I
advanced to the final stage: the personal interview. I had thoroughly prepared, reviewing my
portfolio, practicing responses, and researching the organization. However, during the
interview, I became overwhelmed by the pressure I had placed on myself. Despite knowing
the answers, I faltered, blanked out, and struggled to articulate my thoughts, ultimately failing
to secure the position. While this was a disappointment, it taught me an invaluable lesson
about managing pressure and maintaining composure. I realized that over-preparing to the
point of overwhelm can hinder performance. Since then, I’ve adopted techniques such as
mindfulness and positive visualization to remain calm under pressure. This experience has
shaped my ability to perform better in high-stakes situations, turning a setback into a critical
step toward personal growth.

Q4. Discuss a situation when you were a leader and describe your leadership style?
In my current organization where I work as a UI/UX designer I had the opportunity to lead a
team of interns for a project for the first time in a professional setting. Considering this wasn’t
the first time i had to undertake leaderships responsibilities in a team project because of my
past academic experience and being mentored by other leaders, my qualities have evolved
into being a leader who doesn’t micro manage and lets designers take independent
decisions while being accomodative of every team members opinion. Considering design
can be very subjective it is important to respect opinions and at the same time as a leader
keep in mind what the client is looking for from the output. This comes from a combination of
positive reinforcement and constructive criticism instead of only pointing out the “flaws” in a
design.

Q5. Describe a situation where you have worked well in a team. and how do you
manage conflict in a team?

Q6. How do you deal with criticism?
I believe, In design, criticism is an integral part of the process as it is highly subjective.
Everyone from clients to the design leads have a different perspective on what makes a
design “good” or effective and hence, criticism is a window into their vision from the design
and priorities.


--- Page 75 ---
When I receive feedback, my first step is to listen carefully and seek to understand the
underlying goals or concerns. Often, it’s not just about the specific critique but about what
they’re trying to achieve through the design. I make it a point to ask clarifying questions to
ensure I fully grasp their expectations.

For example, in a project I worked on during college, I jumped to an output fairly quickly
based on my initial research and ideas. However, my mentor advised me to engage with
more stakeholders in the ecosystem to gain a broader perspective and ensure the design
was more informed and aligned with real needs. Taking that feedback, I revisited the
research phase, spoke to additional stakeholders, and ended up with a far more effective
and well-rounded design.

1. Describe how receiving a Postgraduate Scholarship would make a difference to you
