truly grateful for the opportunity to be considered and excited about the possibilities this support could
unlock.

2. Tell us how you intend to use the qualification you will be gaining to support your future plans *
A course in service design will allow me to see projects a lot more holistically and assess project outcomes
more practically rather than being overambitious about it. A course in service design will allow me to
understand how to blend strategy, technology, and design in a manner that makes an impact on a business.
Specifically, I aim to work on service ecosystems that address pressing socio-economic inequities by co-
creating accessible services for marginalized communities - such as affordable healthcare frameworks or skill-
building digital platforms for rural artisans, inspired by my earlier work in Assam. I am also open to working on
digital products like fintech solutions, or interactive educational platforms. My long-term vision is to become
an expert in human-centric design by creating versatile design solutions that have high utility, speak to the
audience, and help achieve the underlying business objectives.

3. Describe your background and your academic experience *
My Bachelor’s degree from India’s premier design school, National Institute of Design (NID), has given my
design thought, creativity, and critical thinking a decisively humane and people-oriented direction. It
introduced me to three facets of design: systems design, service design, and interaction design – each with its
unique applications that called for deeper exploration. The systems approach calls for looking at a problem
area holistically before identifying an intervention type, service design relies on solving design problems with
the aid of techno-digital interventions, whereas interaction design sheds light on making such designs more
user-centric.
Throughout my academic journey, I have maintained a consistently rising scorecard and earned the Highest
Scorer award in my graduating cohort. But while my GPA of 8.6 in my Bachelor's in Industrial Design provides a
glimpse into my academic progress, I believe the essence of design goes beyond conventional grading metrics
and is demonstrated through practical application and impact.
As I took on design assignments in college, I increasingly saw that it also had the unique power to effect social
change. What reinforced this was my on-site graduation project in collaboration with Grant Thornton, where I
worked with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive endangered Japi crafts. Immersing myself in
the local culture deepened my understanding of community-driven design and the impact it can have on the
artisan as well as a customer. In another project, I redesigned construction barricades to improve safety for
pedestrians during peak traffic hours. I used behavioral mapping to understand challenges around their
installation, inconveniences faced by Rickshaw and Two-Wheeler drivers navigating around them, or


--- Page 79 ---
pedestrians crossing near construction zones. After a few rounds of on-ground testing of the full-scale
prototype, I succeeded in designing a lightweight, highly visible barricade that was easy to install, maintain,
and store.
From developing a mechanical tool for garland weaving that improved the efficiency and comfort of flower
vendors to creating an educational activity kit exploring self-identity for children (the latter is selected for
presentation and publication at the International Conference on Research into Design - ICoRD’25), all my
projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, community, and sustainability.
From a bird’s eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility, and
sustainability. This understanding has been further strengthened through my professional experiences - at
Thinkonomers (HDL), where I learned about the importance of user feedback in the design process; Pichkaari
Design Studio, where I worked on rebranding projects and developed user flow enhancement prototypes;
Grant Thornton; and finally, in my current role as a UX/UI designer at BRND Studio, where I handle end-to-end
design for SaaS dashboards, educational platforms, and teaching tools. These experiences have ingrained in
me that user experience should be the top priority in the design process and helped shape my own design
philosophy, which is based on creating superlative, easy-to-use experiences for modern spaces, both virtual
and real. For example, in my initial projects, I dove headfirst into design, but with experience and trial and
error, I realized that logic in design will hold little value unless it is thoughtfully integrated with an intuitive
understanding of how it will be used by humans.
4. Describe how you would use the qualification to address challenges faced by society in your home
country, upholding the values of social justice and environmental stewardship *
Service design has the potential to transform how services are delivered and experienced in India, making
them more inclusive, efficient, and sustainable. Growing up in a country with immense human capital, I have
often observed that while there is no shortage of hard work, the lack of structured and intuitive systems often
prevents that effort from translating into real impact. Whether in businesses, public services, or community-
driven initiatives, the challenge isn’t just about working harder—it’s about working smarter.
One of the areas where this imbalance is most visible is in traditional craftsmanship and small-scale industries.
Many artisans and local producers possess incredible skills, but without well-designed systems to connect
them to the right markets, their work often goes unrecognized or undervalued. Service design can help create
ecosystems that bridge this gap—by streamlining supply chains, improving access to technology, and ensuring
that these businesses operate sustainably while still preserving cultural heritage. I saw this firsthand while
working with artisans, where simply restructuring the way their products were positioned and marketed made
a tangible difference in their livelihoods.
Beyond small businesses, service design has the power to improve workplace experiences at a larger scale. In
many growing organizations, I have noticed how uneven workload distribution and inefficient workflows can
lead to burnout, disengagement, and reduced productivity. Instead of adopting rigid structures modeled after
international firms, businesses in India need solutions that are tailored to their unique challenges. Service
design offers a way to rethink these systems—balancing workloads, redefining roles, and ensuring that
employees and customers alike benefit from a more thoughtful and intuitive approach.
Public services, too, stand to benefit immensely from service design. Long queues at hospitals, complex
government procedures, and inaccessible infrastructure are daily struggles for many people. Simple yet
effective redesigns of these systems—whether through digital interventions, process restructuring, or better
information accessibility—can make these essential services more humane and user-friendly.


--- Page 80 ---
Equally important is the role of service design in promoting environmental sustainability. India’s rapid
economic growth has often come at the expense of ecological well-being. By embedding sustainable practices
into everyday services—whether in waste management, urban planning, or responsible consumption—service
design can ensure that progress does not come at the cost of the planet.
Ultimately, service design is about creating experiences that work seamlessly for people, whether in business,
governance, or daily life. India’s potential is vast, and by prioritizing thoughtful, human-centered solutions, we
can build systems that not only function efficiently but also uphold values of social justice, dignity, and
sustainability.

University of Washington - Personal Statement 01

How have your prior activities and experiences (e.g., things you have done at school, in the workplace, in
your community, and/or extracurricular activities) prepared you for this program and this field? Please
provide specific examples. [600 words maximum]

Growing up in Bangalore, life unfolded in straight lines and stark edges, where every structure was a servant of
purpose, and aesthetics took a backseat to utility. It wasn’t until I wandered through the vibrant streets of
Barcelona during a trip in high school that I discovered the quiet poetry of aesthetics in public spaces. This
revelation stirred a deep admiration within me during those early years. My ensuing creative practice has been
shaped by my undergraduate studies in Industrial Design and diverse professional experiences, deepening my
understanding of how design can bridge the gap between function and form, utility and beauty.

I pursued my Bachelor’s degree from India’s premier design school, the National Institute of Design (NID),
where my design thinking and creativity took a decisively humane and people-oriented direction. During my
time at NID, I was introduced to three facets of design: systems design, service design, and interaction design.
These areas provided me with a holistic perspective on design and offered deep insights into how we can
tackle problems creatively, using diverse approaches. The systems approach taught me to look at a problem
area in its entirety before identifying potential interventions, while service design focused on solving problems
through technological interventions. Interaction design highlighted the importance of user-centricity, making
designs intuitive and accessible.

In college, I took on multiple design assignments that highlighted the power of design in making a tangible
impact on society. One such project was my graduation assignment with Grant Thornton, where I worked with
indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive endangered Japi crafts. Immersing myself in the local
culture gave me a deeper understanding of community-driven design, and I saw firsthand how design could
benefit both artisans and customers. Additionally, in another project, I redesigned construction barricades to
improve safety for pedestrians during peak traffic hours. Using behavioral mapping to understand the
challenges faced by pedestrians and drivers, I designed a lightweight, highly visible barricade that was easy to
install, maintain, and store after a few rounds of on-ground testing.

Another project involved designing a mechanical tool to improve the efficiency and comfort of garland
weaving for flower vendors in my hometown Bengaluru. The manual weaving process caused repetitive strain
injuries for many vendors. By using a participatory design approach, I co-created a tool that alleviated the
physical strain and increased the productivity of the weavers. This experience further strengthened my belief
in design as a catalyst for positive change.

Beyond these community-based projects, I worked on tech-intensive projects as well. For instance, I analyzed
STI management practices and barriers restricting access to services among female sex workers (FSWs) in
Bhopal. I employed snowball sampling techniques to locate their work zones and, using methods like GIGA
mapping user journeys, developed a Trigger and Response Model to better interpret the data. The project led
to recommendations for community-centric support systems such as mobile clinics and awareness programs.
Another project involved creating an educational activity kit to help children explore self-identity. By utilizing
user journey mapping, participatory testing, and iterative prototyping, I built a tool to help children
understand and express their feelings, which was later presented and published at the International
Conference on Research into Design (ICoRD’25).

All these experiences have shaped my design philosophy, which now prioritizes user experience in every
project. At Thinkonomers (HDL), Pichkaari Design Studio, and my current role as a UX/UI designer at BRND


--- Page 82 ---
Studio, I have learned to embrace user feedback as a crucial part of the design process. These roles taught me
the importance of integrating logic and user understanding, which have refined my ability to deliver practical
solutions that resonate with real users. Whether working on SaaS dashboards or educational platforms, I now
approach every project with a balance of creativity, usability, and empathy.

University of Washington - Personal Statement 02

Please describe your particular interest in Human Centered Design & Engineering, and explain what specific
elements of the program make it a good fit for you. How will this program assist you in your longer-term
goals? Please provide specific examples. [600 words maximum]
From a bird’s eye perspective, all my projects have reiterated the interconnection of design, utility, and
sustainability. These principles have inspired my interest in pursuing Human Centered Design & Engineering
(HCDE), where I hope to deepen my expertise and apply my skills in designing for real-world challenges. The
driving force behind my desire to study HCDE is my interest in blending design, technology, and social impact—
areas that align perfectly with my long-term career goals.
After completing my graduate studies, I aim to work as a User Experience (UX) Researcher or Innovation
Strategist with creative firms such as Accenture, Anthro-Tech, Inc., or IDEO. I am passionate about working on
service ecosystems that address pressing socio-economic inequities. For example, I am interested in designing
accessible services for marginalized communities, such as affordable healthcare frameworks or digital platforms
for skill-building among rural artisans. These goals are inspired by my earlier project in Assam, where I worked
with artisans to develop community-driven solutions. Additionally, I am open to contributing to digital products
like fintech solutions or interactive educational platforms, using my design background to create user-friendly,
impactful tools.
The Human Centered Design & Engineering program at the University of Washington offers the ideal
environment for me to cultivate my skills and achieve these goals. I am particularly drawn to the Accessibility
and Inclusive Design and Usability Studies modules, which emphasize design thinking and the practical
application of industry-relevant tools. The program’s emphasis on practical learning through industry
collaborations, live projects, and its state-of-the-art MakeLab, The Momentary Experience Lab, and Inclusive
Design Lab excites me as they provide an opportunity to experiment with emerging technologies such as high-
end VR systems and 3D printers.
Moreover, the University of Washington’s collaborative atmosphere, which includes industry events and
Capstone Showcases, will provide me with valuable opportunities to engage with like-minded peers and
professionals. By working on team-based innovations, I will be able to contribute to contemporary social and
environmental challenges, while gaining hands-on experience in developing solutions. I am excited to make the
most of these opportunities, knowing that they will shape me into an industry-ready professional, equipped with
the skills necessary to achieve my long-term goals.
In sum, the HCDE program will allow me to deepen my understanding of design principles while enabling me to
apply them to create accessible, impactful solutions. By joining this program, I will be positioned to make a
meaningful contribution to the field of human-centered design and engineering, helping to shape the future of
design for social good.

Social Action Impact Award in Human Centered Design & Engineering -
The Social Action Impact Award supports students with a demonstrated commitment to diversity,
equity, and inclusion. It is the Donor’s hope that this Fund will inspire students to engage with
their communities and/or in equity-related work to advance systems and technologies that create
accessible, sustainable, and prosperous futures. Applicants should write a personal statement (500
words max.) describing their contributions as they relate to these areas.

I’m Rachit Singhi, an industrial designer passionate about creating thoughtful, impactful design
experiences and solutions. As I worked on co-curricular design assignments during undergraduate
studies, I discovered that apart from utility and aesthetics, design also had the power to effect social
change. One project that truly reinforced this was my on-site research with Grant Thornton, where I
worked with indigenous bamboo artisans from Assam to revive the endangered Japi craft. It showed
me how designers can breathe new life into products and services, creating self-sustaining and
equitable solutions for marginalised stakeholders.

This, and many more such experiences, have laid my foundation for a broader design practice rooted
in empathy. Since then, my work has engaged a wide range of diverse communities — from working
with transgender and STI patients to children with disabilities and senior citizens — each project has
aimed at reiterating the interconnection of design with diversity, equity, and inclusion. From
designing a tool to ease physical strain and improve productivity for traditional garland weavers to
using behavioural mapping to create safer, more visible construction barricades, I have understood
how participatory design can directly enhance dignity, productivity, and livelihood for underserved
communities whose challenges are often not easily visible.

Additionally, I have worked on research-intensive projects too, helping me analyse and create
impactful design suggestions from a systemic point of view. By studying the barriers to STI
healthcare access among female sex workers in Bhopal, the studies could unveil recommendations
on community-centric support systems, inclusive infrastructure, and accessible awareness
programmes. In another, I designed an educational activity kit to help children understand and
respond to inappropriate touch, later presented at the International Conference on Research into
Design (ICoRD’25).

My myriad set of formative learnings has coalesced into my passion for making a career in human-
centred design and user experience research. I have come to appreciate that product design, albeit a
creative process, requires conversance with software design tools, user-centric methodologies, and
an allied understanding of socio-economic dynamics and business management to truly understand
and deliver the consumer's ask. Inspired by my prior work in Assam, I hope to create frameworks like
skill-building and business training digital platforms for tribal artists or reasonably priced and
accessible healthcare models for communities like female sex workers.
Through several projects, I have understood that design has a unique ability to identify problems, craft
solutions, and create smooth, user-centred experiences. The programme’s scope of experiential
learning through industry collaborations, live projects, and its state-of-the-art Value Sensitive Design
Lab and the DO-IT Centre excites me, as they provide an opportunity to work on people-oriented
projects. This scholarship will empower me to focus fully on developing scalable, inclusive


--- Page 87 ---
technologies that serve those too often overlooked. With my foundation in socially engaged design
and my drive to address systemic inequities, I am committed to becoming a leader who uses design
not just to solve problems — but to reshape systems by creating high-utility, user-oriented solutions
fit for both corporate goals and society's demands.

- THEME_TRIGGERS dict maps 8 themes to keyword/phrase triggers:
  Reliability & failures, Performance & responsiveness, Delight & advocacy,
  Findability & navigation, Collaboration friction, Notification overload,
  Documentation & clarity, Mobile/density/ergonomics
- Thematic_result() function with full docstring; keyword mode vs themes mode
- Uses word-boundary matching for single-word triggers (e.g. "find" ≠ "findings")

Key bugs fixed (documented in week3.md Attempt 02):
- ValueError from non-numeric experience_years ("fifteen" as string)
- Sorting bug returning lowest satisfaction instead of highest

--- WEEK 7 — FIGMA USER FEEDBACK DATASET ---

File: Week 7/week7_figma_feedback.csv (22 rows)

Assignment context: Curated public Figma community feedback on UI3 migration and editor UX.

Sources:
- UI3 migration thread: 20 comments (March–April 2025)
- Editor UX critique thread: 2 comments (Sept–Nov 2025)

Theme frequency (keyword scan of content):
- UI3 mentioned: 12 | UI2 mentioned: 12 | "slow": 4 | panel layout: 4
- forced migration / no choice: 2+ | accessibility: 2 | muscle memory: 1
- performance issues: 1

Representative complaints:
- Forced UI3 migration removes user choice; UI2 was still available until deprecation
- Workflow slowdown, lost muscle memory, buried tools (intersect/union in submenu)
- Visually noisy right panel, grey input backgrounds, hard-to-see checkboxes
- Bottom panel blocks laptop secondary screen workflows
- Library publish button disappears instead of disabling when errors exist
- Component update success toast appears before update completes

Top-voted comment (111 helpful votes, OnlyRodas, April 2025):
"I can't believe Figma is forcing this change on us. UI3 is, without a doubt, the most
unfriendly design possible... Figma was supposed to be about flexibility and user-centered
design—so why ignore the feedback of those who actually use the platform every day?"

Martin Wermers-Pauly editor critique (Sept 2025) — five UX failures in Figma itself:
hidden unlabeled icons, library publish UX, premature success toasts, duplicate file tabs,
inconsistent CMD-D duplicate behavior for components.

--- WEEK 8 — MP2a DECLARATION (FULL TEXT) ---

# MP2a declaration

## 1. Problem

People who want to creatively engage with the night sky have no tool that lets them invent personal constellations from imagination and see them mapped onto real stars — so this tool lets anyone draw a shape using **straight-line segments** on a sketch pad, have **each vertex automatically snapped** to the nearest real catalog star, and receive an **AI-generated name** for the constellation they just created.

## 2. Audience

The primary users are the **general public**, **astronomy hobbyists**, and **artists** — people who find the night sky beautiful and want a low-friction, playful, visually rich way to interact with real stellar data without needing any astronomical background. **Educators and science communicators** are a welcome secondary audience but not the primary framing — the tool is built for **curiosity and creative expression** first.

## 3. Data

The tool uses the **HYG v4.2** star catalog pre-filtered to **~9,000 naked-eye-visible stars** (`mag < 6.5`), prepared by a Python script (`prepare_stars.py`) and hosted as **`stars_visible.csv`** with fields: `id`, `ra`, `dec`, `mag`, `ci`, `spect`, `lum`, `proper`, `dist`, `x`, `y`, `z` — loaded **client-side** in the browser; user-generated constellation names are **optionally** checked for global uniqueness against a **Supabase `used_names`** table, but **full constellation records are not persisted**.

## 4. Track

**Design track** — the product is experienced entirely through its interface: the immersive galaxy star map, the straight-line sketch pad, the snap-to-star result projected onto real stars, the **2D/3D toggle**, and the **PNG export**. The computation (vertex normalisation, nearest-neighbour snap, Claude API naming call) is invisible infrastructure in service of the creative experience.

## 5. Platform

**Lovable** — with the **Claude API** called via Lovable's built-in edge function support for AI naming, and **optional Supabase** for a lightweight used-names registry only.

## 6. Rationale

The product's value is almost entirely in how it looks and feels — an immersive **galaxy aesthetic** in **Roboto Mono**, a smooth draw-to-constellation experience, and a beautiful **2D/3D result** — which makes Lovable the right choice because the interface is the product and Lovable deploys a polished React frontend faster than any alternative. The **nearest-neighbour snap** across ~9,000 stars runs fast enough **client-side in JavaScript** without any compute backend, so there is no technical argument for Bolt at this data scale.

--- REPO PROJECT_CONTEXT.md (FULL) ---

# Project context — HCDE 530 (Rachit)

Human-readable brief for collaborators, reviewers, and coding assistants.  
*Derived from a short interview; update this file if your situation changes.*

## Purpose

- **Primary use:** Course **assignments** for HCDE 530.
- **End goal:** A **clear repository of code** (and related artifacts) you can point to after the quarter.

## Audience

- **Readers:** **Faculty** and **peers**.
- **Organization:** **`Week N/`** folders so work is easy to scan week by week.

## Role and perspective

- **Background:** Human-centered design practitioner (**UX research** and **UX design**), **not** a software engineer.
- **Comment voice:** Prefer **many short comments** framed as a researcher/designer would explain them—not low-level syntax tutorials.
- **Balance:** When both fit, split about **50/50** between:
  - **Research framing** (what the data represents, what you’d trust for a decision, limitations, rigor).
  - **Design framing** (users, flows, implications for the product, handoff or critique).

## Constraints and engineering style

- **Libraries:** No course requirement to use specific stacks; choose what fits the assignment.
- **Complexity:** **Don’t over-engineer.** Favor small, readable scripts that match the assignment scope.
- **Dependencies:** Prefer **standard library** when reasonable (e.g. `csv`, `pathlib`); add third-party packages only when they clearly help.

## Data and week-folder contents

- **Data:** Expect a **mix** of **demo/synthetic** examples and **assignment-appropriate** “real-style” datasets.
- **Artifacts per week:** In addition to code, you may keep **CSVs**, **screenshots**, **HTML summaries/dashboards**, and similar outputs **alongside** scripts in the same `Week N/` folder when the assignment calls for it.

## Git and privacy (current default)

- **Current stance:** For this coursework repo, you’re comfortable **committing and pushing** what you need for assignments (including many artifacts above).
- **Future caveat:** If you later work with **identifiable participants**, **employer-confidential** material, or **unreleased products**, revisit what belongs in a **public** repo; use redaction, synthetic substitutes, or a private store outside Git.

## What “good collaboration” looks like here

- Explain **why** a step exists in HCD terms, not only **what** the code does.
- Keep folder and file names **predictable** within each week.
- After **adding, removing, or moving** tracked files, refresh the directory tree in `.cursorrules` (see repo root `.cursorrules` or run `python3 scripts/refresh_cursorrules_tree.py`).

--- MP1 COMPETENCY CLAIMS (FULL — MP1/mp1.md) ---

MP1: Competency Claims

C5: Data Analysis with Pandas

The foundation of this project was a pandas analysis of the HYG v4.2 star catalog (119,626 real stars) that used all five operations from class to answer three connected questions about how stars can be grouped into constellation-like structures.

The most instructive moment wasn't running the operations, it was deciding which one to reach for each sub-question. Filtering with `df[df['mag'] < 6.5]` reduced the catalog to ~9,000 naked-eye-visible stars before any clustering happened, which was a consequential choice: running K-Means on all 119,626 stars would have produced clusters dominated by catalog stars too faint to see, making the constellation generator meaningless in practice. `df.isnull().sum()` revealed that roughly 3,000 stars were missing spectral class data, a gap I would not have noticed from `df.head()` alone, which directly changed how Chart 3 was scoped: it uses a filtered subset rather than the full visible population, and the interpretation cell explains why. `groupby('cluster')['mag'].median()` showed that the brightness distribution in every cluster follows the same right-skewed pattern, a dense IQR of dim filler stars near mag 5–6 and a short left whisker reaching 2–4 unusually bright outliers, which answered Q2 not just for one cluster but consistently across all 15. That consistency was the actual finding: it's a structural property of the night sky, not a coincidence of one patch.

The learning I took from this: pandas operations are diagnostic tools, not formatting steps. Running `describe()` before writing any visualization code caught the 100,000-parsec sentinel distance value that would have produced impossible outlier points in the 3D chart and made it look broken for reasons that would have been hard to diagnose after the fact.

**Evidence:** `a5_analysis.ipynb` and `mp1b_visualizations.ipynb` (Section 2, Data Profile and Section 3, Analysis).

C6: Data Visualization

Each of the three charts in this project was chosen using the Abela Chart Chooser framework, matching the data structure and question to a chart family before writing any code, rather than defaulting to a generic bar or scatter and adjusting from there.

Chart 1 (Felis, 2D sky scatter) is a *Relationship* chart: it shows the angular relationship between nine real HYG catalog stars in RA/Dec space, connected to form a cat-face outline. The critical design decision was choosing a 2D sky projection over the 3D Cartesian plot I originally built. The 3D version ran first, and the cat was unrecognizable, because the nine anchor stars sit at real distances between 49 pc and 411 pc, so the connecting lines stretched along the line of sight into an unreadable diagonal slab. Switching to 2D wasn't a compromise; it's the correct representation for the question being asked, because constellations are inherently 2D line-of-sight patterns from Earth's vantage point. I kept the 3D version in the notebook as Chart 1b to show that contrast honestly rather than hiding the failed experiment.

Chart 2 (brightness box plot) is a *Distribution* chart: I needed to see the full distribution shape, median, spread, and outliers, for 15 groups simultaneously, to test whether the bright-anchor pattern held consistently or only appeared in a few clusters. A bar chart of means would have hidden the outliers that were the actual finding. A histogram per cluster would have produced 15 stacked plots that couldn't be compared. The horizontal box plot with navy diamond overlays at mag < 3.0 made the gap between filler stars and bright anchors immediately readable across all 15 groups at once. Sorting by median magnitude rather than cluster ID was a second design decision that made the pattern visible, alphabetical sorting initially made the gradient invisible.

Chart 3 (spectral composition, 100% stacked bar) is a *Composition* chart: I was asking about relative share of spectral classes within each cluster, not absolute counts. A regular stacked bar would have conflated cluster size with composition, since the 15 clusters vary in how many stars they contain. The 100% normalized version isolates the compositional question. Sorting clusters by combined O+B hot-star share descending, rather than by cluster ID, revealed a genuine gradient from 14.7% to 44.1% that would have been invisible in arbitrary order.

**Evidence:** `mp1b_visualizations.ipynb` (Section 3, Analysis), chart justification in each intro cell, and Section 5, Process.

C7: Critical Evaluation and Professional Judgment

Three specific moments in this project required catching AI output before acting on it, and each one had real consequences for the analysis.

The first was the dataset itself. I started with three CSV files and asked Claude to merge them into a unified star catalog. The file came back complete-looking, every row filled, no obvious errors. But Claude flagged that the three files shared no common key, meaning the physical properties (temperature, luminosity, spectral class) had been bootstrap-sampled and randomly reassigned to positions they had no real connection to. A hot blue star might end up at a cool red star's coordinates. Rather than accept the output because it looked finished, I treated the flag as a real concern: a constellation generator built on fabricated correlations would render stars in the wrong colors at the wrong sizes for their positions, silently. I evaluated the recommended alternative (HYG), spot-checked it against published values for Sirius and Betelgeuse to verify the columns were correct, and discarded the merged file entirely. The final notebook uses HYG throughout.

The second was the 3D cat chart. Cursor produced it at my request and it ran without errors, but when I looked at the output, the cat shape was unrecognizable. The connecting lines stretched diagonally rather than tracing a face because the anchor stars sat at very different real distances. I documented this in Section 5 and kept the failed chart in the notebook as Chart 1b rather than hiding it, because the failure itself is the finding: constellation shapes are projection artifacts, not physical structures in space. Showing the failure honestly is more informative than only showing the version that worked.

The third was the "Build Your Own Constellation" interactive section. An earlier version used a Plotly `FigureWidget` with `dragmode='drawline'` and a live widget button, Cursor built it correctly for a local environment. But I knew from the submission requirement that the notebook needed to render on GitHub, where Jupyter widget bridges don't work. I caught that before submitting and rewrote the section as a code-driven dictionary-edit pattern that works in every viewer, including static GitHub rendering. The criterion I used: "would a grader opening this on GitHub see broken widgets or working output?"

The pattern across all three: AI tools produce structurally correct output that is substantively wrong, a complete-looking CSV with fabricated correlations, a chart that runs but is unreadable, an interactive widget that works locally but breaks on submission. The defense in each case was a concrete verification test, not a general sense that something might be off.

**Evidence:** Section 5, Process in `mp1b_visualizations.ipynb`, and the "Build Your Own Constellation" section explaining the widget decision.

C2: Code Literacy and Documentation

The notebook contains working docstrings, inline comments that explain design decisions rather than code mechanics, and a written process narrative that makes the reasoning visible to a non-technical reader.

The `to_xyz()` function has a docstring that specifies what it takes (RA in hours, Dec in degrees, distance in parsecs) and what it returns (Cartesian x, y, z in parsecs), the unit specification matters because the HYG catalog stores RA in hours, not degrees, and a caller who got that wrong would produce rotated positions without any error message. The `view_for_pad()` function's inline comment explains *why* the x-axis range is reversed (`x reversed: RA increases to the LEFT`), that's the standard astronomical convention, and without the comment a reader would think the range was a bug. The Sol exclusion in Chart 2 has a comment explaining the specific problem: its magnitude of -26.7 is technically below the mag < 6.5 filter but would compress the entire x-axis by 30 magnitudes if included, making all other stars invisible. That's not what the code does, it's why the code does it, which is the distinction C2 is asking for.

The Section 5 process narrative functions as documentation for a non-technical reader: it explains what the dataset problem was, what the 3D chart failure revealed, and why the interactive widget was rebuilt, all without assuming the reader can follow the code. A colleague who had never seen the notebook could read Section 5 and understand the three biggest design decisions made during the project and why they went the direction they did.

The learning I took from this: comments that explain *why* are qualitatively different from comments that explain *what*. "Filter to mag < 6.5" describes what the code does. "Filter to naked-eye-visible stars so the clustering reflects stars people can actually see" explains why the threshold is where it is and what would break if you changed it. Writing the second kind forces you to understand the code well enough to defend it, not just run it.

**Evidence:** Docstrings on `to_xyz()` and `view_for_pad()`, inline comments throughout `mp1b_visualizations.ipynb`, and Section 5, Process.

--- MP2 REFLECTION (FULL — MP2/reflection.md) ---

# MP2 Reflection

## What did you build?

Twinkle is a creative web tool that lets anyone invent their own constellation from imagination and see it mapped onto real stars in the night sky. It loads 8,834 real stars from the HYG astronomical catalog — each one sized by apparent magnitude and colored by its B-V color index so the star field looks like the actual night sky. Users draw a shape on a sketch pad using straight-line segments, and when they submit, each vertex of their drawing snaps to the nearest real catalog star. The result is their invented shape rendered as a glowing constellation overlay on the star map. Three input methods exist: drawing manually on the pad, typing a description and having AI fetch a matching icon shape from Iconify's library of 275,000 icons, or drawing with a finger in front of the laptop camera using MediaPipe hand tracking. Once a constellation is drawn, users describe it in plain text and a Groq-powered AI generates a mythological-sounding name. The constellation can be viewed in 2D or 3D, and downloaded as a PNG. On mobile, a rear-camera AR mode uses device orientation to overlay the constellation on the real night sky.

For a collaborator outside the course: a browser-based creative instrument where `StarMap.tsx` renders `public/stars_visible.csv` on Canvas, `SketchHUD.tsx` handles drawing and IMAGINE, and `/api/name-constellation` calls Groq server-side. It turns MP1's analytical question — can a user shape map to real stars? — into an interactive experience.

## What decisions did you make?

The original MP2a declaration proposed full Supabase persistence for constellation records and freehand curve drawing. Both changed. Supabase was scoped down to an optional name registry only — the core experience works entirely without a database, which made the tool simpler and more reliable. Freehand curves were replaced with straight-line polyline segments because constellations are traditionally straight lines between stars, and the snap-to-star algorithm works much more cleanly with discrete vertices than with continuous curves. The platform choice was Lovable because the interface is the product — an immersive galaxy aesthetic in Roboto Mono that needed to look and feel right immediately, which Lovable handles faster than building from scratch. Groq was chosen over Claude for the naming feature because it offers a genuinely free tier with no credit card required, which mattered for a deployed public tool.

Data came from HYG v4.2 filtered in `Week 8/prepare_stars.py` with precomputed x/y/z for `StarMap3D.tsx`. Canvas over SVG for per-star glow; Iconify SVG paths over LLM coordinates for reliable shapes. Cutting Supabase was the hardest call — the product is creation and download, not a shared registry.

## What would you do differently?

Two specific things. First, I would define the shape imagination feature more narrowly from the start. The feature went through four failed approaches before landing on the Iconify SVG solution — LLM coordinate generation, Quick Draw dataset with CORS issues, hardcoded template lists, and few-shot prompting. Defining upfront that the input needed to be an existing visual asset rather than AI-generated geometry would have saved that entire iteration cycle. Second, I would add user location to the AR mode. Right now the device orientation mapping is approximate because it uses compass heading without knowing where on Earth the user is standing, which means the star positions are directionally correct but not precisely aligned. Adding a one-time GPS permission would make the AR astronomically accurate.

I learned that "AI-powered" is not a specification — I needed reliable polyline vertices, not coordinate hallucination. I would write that constraint on day one. For AR, I would add `navigator.geolocation` to `ARSkyView.tsx` and compute local sidereal time so the overlay aligns with the actual sky above the user.

## What does this work demonstrate?

This project demonstrates C6 through the star rendering — real physical properties encoded as visual variables across 8,834 objects. It demonstrates C7 through three documented moments of catching and correcting AI output before it reached users: the coordinate generation failure, the CORS issue with Quick Draw, and the finger tracking sensitivity calibration. It demonstrates C8 through a fully deployed tool at a custom domain with a complete user flow that works end to end on both desktop and mobile. The finger drawing feature using MediaPipe hand tracking and the AR sky view using device orientation both go beyond the assignment minimum and reflect genuine product thinking about what would make the experience feel magical rather than just functional.

C6 is in `StarMap.tsx` — `mag` and `ci` as visual encodings, 3D revealing distance distortion. C7 is rejecting bad AI defaults: Iconify over coordinates, palm-size calibration over fixed thresholds. C8 is Python preprocessing through to Groq on Cloudflare Workers at twinkle.ltd. I came away understanding competencies as decisions embedded in what shipped, not labels added afterward.

# End-of-Quarter Reflection

HCDE 530 | Rachit Singhi

I came in as a UX researcher and designer who could read a chart but could not really build one, and who treated a dataset as a finished object handed to me rather than something I had to interrogate. Looking back through my repo now, the commit history, the weekly notebooks, the two major projects, I can see the actual shape of what changed. It was not that I learned to write a large amount of code. It was that I learned to be the person who decides whether the code, and the data behind it, can be trusted, and who can explain that decision to someone else.

This reflection makes the case for three of the eight domains: C5, C7, and C8. I picked them because together they trace the real arc of my quarter. C5 is learning to ask a dataset a precise question. C7 is learning to not believe an answer just because it looks finished. C8 is learning to ship something a stranger can actually use without me standing next to them. For each one I name the domain, point to specific files and findings you can open and verify in the repo, explain what being able to do it shows about my capability, and say honestly what I learned in the process. I left C2 (Code Literacy and Documentation) out on purpose, even though there is real evidence for it, because my own `Week 3/week3.md` admits my early commit messages such as "Fixed 2 bugs" were too vague, and the git log proves it. I would rather make a strong case for three domains than a partial case for four.

---

## C5 - Data Analysis with Pandas

**Where to look:** `Week 5/MP1/a5_analysis.ipynb`, `MP1/mp1.md`, the C5 write-up in `Week 5/MP1/week5.md`, and the operations table in `README.md`.

For MP1 I worked with the HYG v4.2 star catalog, which holds about 119,626 real stars, each with position (`ra`, `dec`), distance, apparent and absolute magnitude, a B-V color index (`ci`), spectral class (`spect`), luminosity, and constellation. I used pandas to answer three connected questions: can stars be grouped into constellation-like clusters by position, do a few bright stars anchor each cluster, and do different clusters hold physically different kinds of stars. I used all five operations we covered in class, `head`, `info`, `isnull`, filtering, and `value_counts`, plus `groupby` repeatedly, but the part I am genuinely proud of is not that I called them. It is that I can explain why I reached for each specific one to answer each specific sub-question, which is the difference between running pandas and doing analysis with it.

The clearest example is the filtering step, `df[df['mag'] < 6.5]`, which reduced the catalog to roughly 9,000 naked-eye-visible stars before any clustering happened. That looks like a one-line throwaway, but it was the decision the entire project rested on. Magnitude runs backward, lower numbers are brighter, and 6.5 is roughly the limit of what a human eye can see in a dark sky. If I had run K-Means on all 119,626 rows, the clusters would have been dominated by stars too faint to ever see with the naked eye, and a "constellation generator" built on invisible stars would be meaningless in practice. So choosing where to put that filter was really choosing what question I was answering. I learned that the most consequential line of an analysis is often not the modeling step, it is the quiet filter near the top that defines the population, and that a reader deserves to know why the threshold sits where it does.

A second moment came from `df.isnull().sum()`, which showed me that roughly 3,000 stars were missing spectral class data. I would never have noticed that from `df.head()` alone, because the first five rows looked perfectly clean. That single count changed how I scoped the spectral composition question: instead of quietly running an analysis over thousands of blanks and reporting a confident but corrupted result, I worked on a filtered subset and said so in the notebook. What I learned here is that missing data is not an edge case to handle at the end, it is information about the dataset itself, and checking for it early is how you avoid publishing a clean-looking number that is actually built on holes.

The real finding came from `groupby('cluster')` on the magnitude column. Across nearly every cluster, the brightness distribution had the same right-skewed shape: a dense band of dim filler stars near magnitude 5 to 6, and a short tail reaching two to four genuinely bright anchor stars. The point was not that one cluster behaved this way, it was that almost all of them did. That consistency is the actual result, because it suggests a structural property of how the night sky is organized rather than a coincidence in one lucky patch. Being able to see that required grouping and then comparing across groups, not just describing one. I learned that the interesting answer is frequently a pattern that holds across many groups, and that aggregation is how you make that pattern visible instead of guessing at it.

One more piece of evidence is small but it captures the whole mindset. Running `describe()` before writing any visualization code caught a 100,000-parsec sentinel distance value sitting in the data. Left alone, that placeholder would have thrown an impossible outlier point into a later 3D chart and made the chart look broken for a reason that is very hard to diagnose after the fact, when you are staring at a finished plot wondering why it is wrong. Catching it at the profiling stage cost me one cell. Catching it after the chart shipped would have cost me an afternoon.

**What this demonstrates and what I learned:** I can take a large, real, imperfect dataset and turn a vague curiosity into a specific, testable question, then choose the pandas operation that answers that exact question rather than running everything and hoping something stands out. More than the syntax, I learned a sequence I now treat as non-negotiable: profile the data before you analyze it, define your population on purpose, check for what is missing, and look for patterns that hold across groups rather than anecdotes from one. That sequence is the difference between a number I would defend in a meeting and a number I just produced.

---

## C7 - Critical Evaluation and Professional Judgment

**Where to look:** `Week 6/MP1/week6.md`, the C7 section of `MP1/mp1.md`, and the three documented moments in `MP2/mp2.md`.

This is the domain where I changed the most, and it shows up in both major projects, which is exactly why I picked it. I do not have one example, I have several, and each one forced an actual decision rather than a vague feeling that something might be off.

The first was the MP1 dataset itself. I began with three CSV files I had pulled together, a large N-body simulation, a sky survey, and a short list of named stars, and I asked Claude to merge them into one unified star catalog. The merged file came back looking perfect: every row populated, no errors, a tidy table. But Claude flagged that the three files shared no common key, which meant the physical properties had been randomly paired to positions they had no real relationship to. A hot blue star could end up sitting at a cool red dwarf's coordinates, which is physically impossible. The output looked complete and was substantively wrong. Instead of accepting it because it was tidy, I treated that flag as a real problem. I looked at the recommended alternative, the HYG database, and before trusting it I spot-checked it against published values for two stars I could verify by hand, Sirius and Betelgeuse, to confirm the columns matched reality. Only then did I throw the synthetic file away and rebuild on HYG. What I learned is that a perfectly formatted table with invented relationships inside it is more dangerous than no table at all, because it fails silently and confidently, and the only defense is to check it against a ground truth I trust.

The second moment was in MP1b. Cursor built me a 3D version of my cat constellation exactly as I asked, using the stars' real distances in Cartesian space, and it ran with no errors at all. But when I looked at it, the cat was unrecognizable. The nine anchor stars sit between 49 and 411 parsecs from Earth, so connecting them in true 3D stretched the outline into a diagonal slab along the line of sight. The code worked and the output was useless. I made two judgment calls. First, I went back to the boring 2D sky projection, because that is genuinely the correct representation for a constellation, which is a line-of-sight pattern from where we stand, not a shape floating in space. Second, I kept the failed 3D version in the notebook as Chart 1b on purpose, sitting next to the version that works, because the failure is itself the finding. I learned that the right fix is sometimes the less impressive one, and that showing a failure honestly teaches the reader more than hiding it. A third, related catch in the same project was noticing that a Plotly drag-to-draw widget ran fine on my machine but would not render on GitHub, where the notebook would actually be graded, so I rewrote it into a pattern that works in any viewer. The test I used was simple: would a grader opening this on GitHub see working output or a broken widget.

MP2 pushed this further, and the honest write-up in `MP2/mp2.md` documents three failures I had to catch and redirect, each of which changed the architecture of a feature. First, I asked Groq to return arrays of x,y coordinates that traced a shape like a cat face, and the output was random lines with no resemblance to anything, even after I added few-shot examples. The root cause, once I named it, was clear: a language model is trained on text, not geometry, so it has no reliable spatial reasoning about coordinate systems. The fix was to stop asking AI to do geometry and use it only for the text matching it is genuinely good at, sending the user's words to the Iconify icon library and parsing the real SVG path that comes back. Second, I tried Google's Quick Draw dataset and hit a CORS wall, then routed around it through an edge function only to find the latency was an unreliable two to eight seconds because the source files are enormous. I judged that a demo that works once is not a feature a user can depend on, and switched to an approach under 500 milliseconds. Third, the MediaPipe finger-drawing feature placed a new vertex on every tiny hand tremor, so I built a calibration step that measures the user's palm size in pixels and scales the movement threshold to how far their hand is from the camera, instead of trusting a single fixed number the tool had picked.

**What this demonstrates and what I learned:** I can tell the difference between output that looks correct and output that is correct, and I can name the concrete test I would use to decide. That is the part of working with AI that a stakeholder actually pays a person for. What I learned, across all of these, is that critical evaluation is not skepticism for its own sake and it is not catching typos. It is knowing what question I am really trying to answer before I accept an answer, separating what a tool is good at from what it merely sounds confident about, and always verifying against either a ground truth or the real deployment context. I learned to ask three questions out of habit now: is this the right tool for this job, is it reliable enough for a real user, and would I put my name on it in front of a client without checking it first.

---

## C8 - Building and Deploying a Complete Tool

**Where to look:** the live site at https://twinkle.ltd, `MP2/mp2.md`, `MP2/reflection.md`, the original declaration in `Week 8/MP2a.md`, the data pipeline in `Week 8/prepare_stars.py`, and the components under `MP2/src/`.

Twinkle is my MP2. It is deployed on a custom domain and anyone with a browser can use it, which is the part that makes it "complete" rather than "working on my machine." The full flow runs end to end: a person draws a shape, each vertex snaps to the nearest real star from the HYG catalog, a Groq-powered AI gives the constellation a name, and they can view it in 2D or 3D and download it as a PNG. On a phone, a rear-camera mode overlays the result on the real night sky. The thing I want to argue is not that it looks nice, it is that it is built as a chain of honest layers, and that I can point to each layer and explain the decision behind it.

The data does not come from the app, it comes from a separate Python script, `Week 8/prepare_stars.py`, and the details there are verifiable. It filters the raw HYG catalog to magnitude under 6.5 and distance greater than 0.01 parsecs, which is how the roughly 119,000-star catalog becomes the about 8,835 stars the site actually loads. It converts right ascension from hours to degrees with `ra_deg = ra_h * 15.0`, because HYG stores RA in hours but the web renderer needs degrees, and getting that one multiplication wrong would silently rotate the entire sky. It precomputes Cartesian `x, y, z` coordinates with a `to_xyz()` function so the browser does not have to do trigonometry for thousands of stars at render time. And it fills missing values with safe defaults through small `_float` and `_int` helpers, so a blank color index becomes 0.0 (white) and a blank spectral class becomes "Unknown" rather than crashing the front end. That preprocessing discipline is the same lesson I first practiced in Week 4, where `export_pexels_to_csv.py` loaded an API key from a `.env` file with `os.environ.get` and dug two levels into the JSON response to pull nested image URLs out of the `src` object. The Week 4 habit of keeping keys out of the code carried directly into MP2, where the Groq API key lives in a Cloudflare Workers edge function rather than the browser, because anything in client code can be read by anyone in their developer tools.

The part I think matters most for this domain is what I deliberately cut. My original declaration is in `Week 8/MP2a.md`, and `MP2/reflection.md` documents the scope decisions I made from it: Supabase persistence was cut entirely, and freehand drawing was replaced with straight-line polyline segments — both verifiable in the repo. The declaration had included an optional Supabase `used_names` table to check new constellation names for global uniqueness; I cut even that, and shipped with no database at all. Adding the Supabase integration, plus the row-level security and authentication it would have pulled in once names were stored, was real work that the core experience, create and download, does not actually need. Cutting a feature I had already named in my own declaration was uncomfortable, and `MP2/reflection.md` says so plainly, but it clarified what the product really is: a creative instrument, not a social platform. I learned that scope is not a list of everything possible, it is a decision about what the product is for, and that shipping the smaller honest version is a stronger move than missing the deadline chasing the bigger one.

I also know exactly where the seam is that I did not finish, and I think naming it is part of shipping. The AR sky view uses the phone's compass heading from `deviceorientationabsolute` but does not use GPS, so the overlay is directionally close but not astronomically exact, because the correct mapping from compass heading to a region of sky depends on where on Earth you are standing and the local time. The fix, which I describe in both `mp2.md` and `reflection.md`, is a one-time `navigator.geolocation` permission and a local sidereal time calculation. I did not pretend the seam was not there, and I can tell you precisely what I would do with another week.

**What this demonstrates and what I learned:** I can scope a real project for a real human-centered use case, make the architecture decisions that keep it working for a stranger and not just on my laptop, secure the parts that need securing, and be honest about the trade-offs. What I learned is that "complete" does not mean every feature from the original plan. It means every step of the user journey works reliably, from a Python preprocessing script, to a static CSV served by the front end, to an API key hidden behind an edge function, to a custom domain a person can open on their phone. I also learned that each layer only works if the one below it is honest about its assumptions, and that knowing exactly where your seams are is what separates a finished project from a project that merely looks finished.

---

## One thing I learned that was not in the objectives

The syllabus taught me tools. The thing I did not expect to learn was how to manage one.

By the end of the quarter I stopped treating AI like an oracle that hands me finished answers and started treating it like a fast, confident junior collaborator that I am responsible for supervising. That sounds like a small reframe, but it changed how I work day to day. I now assume that anything a tool gives me is a draft, and that my actual job is to find the one place it went quietly wrong, because the quiet wrongness is the expensive kind. The merged star CSV taught me this first. It was the moment I really understood that "looks complete" and "is correct" are two different properties, and that only one of them is the tool's responsibility while the other one is mine.

The test I built out of that habit is simple: find one value in the output I can verify independently, check it, and if it's right, trust the pattern. If it's wrong, find out why before going further. That test takes thirty seconds and has saved me hours three separate times this quarter. Before I trust a feature I imagine a real person using it in a worse situation than mine — a shaky hand far from the camera, a phone with no location permission, a notebook opened on GitHub instead of my laptop.

The second thing I learned was more uncomfortable than the AI one because it meant choosing the less impressive option on purpose. When I built the 3D constellation view in MP1b, the result was technically correct and experientially useless. The cat face dissolved when rotated because the anchor stars sit at real distances between 49 and 411 parsecs and the outline only exists when viewed from exactly Earth's angle. The right fix was the 2D sky projection, which is simpler, less dramatic, and actually correct for what a constellation is. I kept the 3D version in the notebook as Chart 1b precisely because the failure is the finding, and hiding it would have been dishonest about what the data shows. What I learned from that is a principle I think applies directly to design work: the right representation for a question is not always the most sophisticated one, and choosing a simpler tool because it fits the problem better is a stronger judgment than choosing a complex one because it demonstrates more skill. In HCD terms, the question "what does this person actually need to understand" should come before "what is the most impressive thing I can build." I knew that as a principle from research. I had not really practiced it as a builder until this quarter.

The third thing I learned was about a category of failure I did not have a name for before this quarter. Every error I had encountered before produced a signal — a crash, a traceback, a broken chart, something visually wrong. What I did not know how to look for was the failure that produces nothing. The three-CSV merge completed without a single warning and returned a perfectly formatted table. The 100,000-parsec distance values in the HYG catalog sat in the dist column looking like normal numbers. The synthetic file's physical properties were correctly typed, correctly formatted, and completely fabricated in terms of which star they actually described. None of these threw an error because none of them were errors in a technical sense. The data was structured correctly. It was just wrong. What I learned to do, slowly and mostly by getting burned, was to build a verification step into my workflow before I trusted any output I had not personally traced from source to result. For the HYG catalog that meant checking Sirius and Betelgeuse against published values before building anything on top of the data. For the dist column it meant running df.describe() before writing a single visualization and asking whether the maximum value made physical sense. For the merged CSV it meant taking the flag seriously rather than dismissing it because the table looked tidy. The habit that came out of all of this is one sentence I now ask before moving forward: could this be wrong in a way that would not tell me. If the answer is yes, I find the check before I trust the output. That question did not come from a slide. It came from several hours of debugging analyses that produced confident wrong answers, from rubric feedback across the quarter pushing me to be specific about what I actually verified rather than what I assumed, and from eventually realizing that the most dangerous kind of error is the one that looks like it is done.

I think this is the most useful thing I am leaving the quarter with, because it transfers far beyond this course. It is not really about stars, or pandas, or Cloudflare Workers. It is a way of working where I stay accountable for the output no matter how it was produced, and where I can explain to a colleague or a stakeholder exactly what I checked, what I chose to trust, and why. For a researcher and designer who is now also someone who builds things, that judgment is worth more than any single tool I picked up, and it is the part of this quarter I expect to still be using years from now.

--- GRADUATION PROJECT (Apunar Japi — Blending Tradition and Innovation) ---
URL: https://rachitsinghi.wixsite.com/my-site-16/graduation-project
NID final year project (6 months); connects to Grant Thornton artisan work and japi craft preservation.

Apunar Japi overview poster ("Blending Tradition and Innovation"):
- Objective: collaborative partnership with bamboo artisans in Barpeta district, Assam
- Goal: co-produced collections (4–10 products each) for middle/upper-middle home decor market
- Aims: financial benefits to artisans, product range diversification, specialized training
  (3 topics), emphasis on empowering women artisans
- Co-creative Venn: Designers (material properties, cluster understanding, market analysis,
  consumer preferences, crafting techniques) × Artisans (marketability, financial stability,
  empowerment, self-dependency, market access, existing skills) → product diversification +
  design training
- Stage 01 Research: bamboo industry India, handicraft interventions, weaving techniques,
  Japi history/evolution/types/traditional use in Assam weddings
- Bamboo facts: 30–100 cm/day growth, up to 36m height, 1–30 cm diameter
- India bamboo industry: 13.96M hectares; 13% of forest area; 15.4% private lands;
  39% of India's bamboo in North East (map included)

Cultivation slide:
- Bamboo rhizome network; new shoots form dense clumps; some species invasive
- Prevention: non-invasive species, contained planting, regular pruning/mowing/digging
- Growth stages illustrated: Initial planting → 1st Spring (up to 4 ft/day) → 2nd Summer →
  Late 1st Spring (60-day cycle) → 3rd Summer (maturity)

Harvest slide:
- Bamboo harvested every 3–5 years depending on product requirements
- Best time: end of rainy season / beginning of dry season
- Harvest identification: young culms smooth shiny green; mature darker with lichens;
  foliage greys with age
- Photo grid: Shoot → Young → Mature → Old culms

Government ecosystem maps (two large systems diagrams):
- Central ministries: DONER, Finance/NABARD (Bamboo Development Fund), Agriculture
  (National Bamboo Mission), Textiles (NHDP, SFURTI, Ambedkar Hastshilp Vikas Yojana)
- Assam state org: Dept of Industries & Commerce (DICC, Swayem, Biponi, Sarothi schemes),
  Handloom/Textiles/Sericulture (PMEGP, ARTFED, AGMC), Cultural Affairs (Guru Shishya
  Parampara), State Bamboo Development Agency
- Commerce: MEIS export scheme for bamboo products
- Tribal Affairs: TRIFED Van Dhan Yojana → Van Dhan Vikas Kendras in Assam

MATHEMATICA / AGRA DASHBOARD — DETAILS FROM CASE STUDY IMAGES
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Project metadata (from "Problem Area" slide):
- Problem: AGRA needed a way to track agricultural progress across Africa using
  complex, scattered data. Leadership lacked an intuitive tool to visualize
  insights and compare performance across regions and variables.
- Duration: 8 Weeks | Project Type: Client based | Project Category: Dashboard Design

Highlights board:
- "Single platform for all data tracking requirements"
- Outcome 1: Design of an Interactive Dashboard — a user-friendly platform for
  tracking progress, identifying gaps, and assessing policy effectiveness.
- Outcome 2: Functional Prototypes — interactive demos to showcase the
  dashboard's capabilities to stakeholders.
- Outcome 3: UI Kit — a design system ensuring brand consistency across digital tools.
- Tagline: "Better alignment = Better funds allocation"
- Collaborated with: Project Managers, Developers, Policy Experts, AGRA researchers.
- Impacts: 300+ everyday users | 11+ African countries rely on it.
- Mathematica brand tagline: "Progress Together"

The product — AGRA DASHBOARD 3.0:
- Subtitle: "Food Systems-led Inclusive Agricultural Transformation in Africa"
- Navigation: AGRA | Corporate | Countries | Business Lines | Explore KPIs
- AGRA Countries: interactive Africa map — "To support the execution of the
  strategy at country level, AGRA is developing country strategies. Choose a
  country to see their performance."
- Three strategy pillars: (1) Empower and build resilience of smallholder
  farmers; (2) Support inclusive markets to improve functional ag systems;
  (3) Build state capability to sustain agricultural transformation.
- AGRA Business Lines stat cards:
  * Inclusive Markets & Trade — 28M farmers targeted with our partners
  * Seed Systems — 10 country seed systems strengthened
  * Youth, Gender, Inclusivity — 10M number of youth reached
  * Policy & State Capability — $3BN leveraged by governments for NAIPS and
    flagships supported by AGRA
  * Sustainable Farming — 40% targeted farmers adopt nutrient-dense or
    climate-smart crop varieties

Corporate screens walkthrough (numbered annotations):
01 — The dashboard landing page shows the organization's overall goals and
     reminds stakeholders of what needs to be achieved.
02 — The corporate KPIs arranged in cards display progress with
     micro-visualizations and a click into detailed views.
03 — Detailed click-ins of KPIs open up graphs in a pop-up window with the
     option to switch views in different comparative parameters
     (e.g. Corporate Youth KPI: Gender / Age disaggregation, Baseline (B),
     Midline (M), Endline (E) trend, country breakdown).
04 — Corporate KPIs shown within specific country pages.
05 — A "whiteboard" feature allowed side-by-side KPI tracking for
     decision-making support and customization ability.
06 — Users could refine datasets by country, crop type, and policy
     interventions, and view the selected KPIs as a graph or a table for
     easier comparison (KPI Comparison: Actuals vs Target, in percentage).

KPI categories visible in UI: Socio Economic Indicators (e.g. number of youth
with enabled or enhanced work opportunities — target 3M; number of youth in
dignified and fulfilling work — target 1.5M); Youth, Gender, Inclusivity
(number of youth reached — target 10M); Policy & State Capability; Inclusive
Market, Trade & Finance; Sustainable Farming.

Country list in dashboard: Burkina Faso, Côte d'Ivoire, Ethiopia, Ghana,
Kenya, Malawi, Mali, Mozambique, Nigeria, Rwanda, Senegal, Tanzania, Togo,
Uganda, Zambia. Region filters: East Africa, West Africa, Southern Africa.

Rwanda country page example: "Improve farmer incomes, create jobs, and
increase exports through improved market linkages and increased
processing/value addition in the Horticulture and Poultry Value Chains."
Farmer KPIs include: % of smallholder households able to deal with and
recover from shocks; smallholder farmer Food Insecurity Experience Scale
(FIES); value of smallholder farmer sales of focus crops; average yields for
focus crops; % adopting sustainable farming or climate-resilient practices;
number of farmers reached (target 1.98M); % adopting nutrient-dense and/or
climate-smart varieties.

Whiteboard KPI table fields: KPI Name, KPI Type (Corporate/Other), Country,
Business Line, Updated on; filters for KPI Type, Country, Business Line,
Demographic, Value Chain, Year; Compare KPIs action; toggle graph/table view.

Wireframe stages documented: Navigation Bar, Landing Page, Corporate Page,
Country Page. Landing targets shown: 15 focus countries, 28M farmers
targeted, 10 seed systems, $3BN, $300M, 30%, 25%, 40%, 12.

Process artifacts: full Figma flow maps (Prototype for Dev, Flexible
Prototype, Simple Click-Through, UX Guidelines; flows for Corporate BME,
Disaggregation, Annual Performance). Hand-drawn notebook sketches exploring
chart types: stacked bar ("each stack is one country coloured differently —
total height gives the aggregated value for that year"), grouped bar chart
(Target vs Actual), line charts (Y1–Y5 actual vs target), bullet charts
(actual depth greyed out or dashed lines to show target), baseline (2023) /
midline (2025) / endline (2027) markers.

INSYNC.AI — DETAILS FROM CASE STUDY IMAGES
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Project metadata (from "Problem Area" slide):
- Problem: Building and customizing chatbots is slow and developer-dependent.
  InSync needed a no-code platform to simplify this process. Our goal was to
  design an intuitive configuration tool that balances power with ease of use.
- Duration: 4 Weeks | Project Type: Client based | Project Category: AI Configuration Tool

Highlights board:
- "Single platform for all chatbot configuration needs"
- Outcome 1: Design of a modular interface ready to scale with future
  features and use cases needed to make the product flexible.
- Outcome 2: Clickable prototype to support development and align product
  vision across teams.
- Outcome 3: UI guidelines aligned for design consistency across the ecosystem.
- Tagline: "No-code setup = Faster deployment and scalability"
- Collaborated with: InSync.AI's leadership, Developers, Research Team,
  Product Managers.
- Impacts: 3x faster chatbot deployment | 40% reduction in support costs.

Product features designed (from final screens):
- Admin Home dashboard with metrics: Webpage Links (5.6M), Chat Opened
  (4,837), Unique User Sessions (3,256), Agent Cost Saved ($1,840), Agent
  Hours Saved (92).
- Knowledge Pool: Links — table of the bot's knowledge files/links (~85
  links) with scan mode (Full Website / Just this URL), status (Indexed,
  Indexing, Failed), tags, and Add Link action. "You can add, update, or
  remove them, starting in Staging and marking for Production after testing."
- Controls → Sensitive Topics — list of topics the bot should consider
  sensitive (e.g. "Queries around students dropping out") plus a disclaimer
  shared alongside answers ("This is an AI generated answer, please make sure
  to cross check for accuracy and quality").
- Integrations — Salesforce, Zendesk, Canvas, Blackboard LMS, Custom
  Integrations. "Integrate systems for seamless handoffs and connect the LMS
  to support students' learning."
- Appearance — "Design what the chatbot will look like on your website,
  including colors, name, and icons used." Basic Settings: Chat Header (brand
  logo, chatbot display name, header color, header text color), Chat Body
  (background color, background gradient color), Chat Button (icon); live
  Preview panel; color picker.
- Customise your Chatbot — "Build the visual identity for your chatbot";
  click-on-elements editing for Logos and Icons, Colors, Rename; tabs for
  Icon and Logo, Header Colors, Background Colors, Display Name.
- Bot's Personality — guide on how answers should be delivered by the bot;
  inputs on tone and how it should address the user (guide points).
- Bot Testing → Chat Bot Script — WebJS snippet to copy into the website
  where the chatbot should appear; Export GTM Tag / Copy Script. "Once
  developed, the chatbot code will be ready for easy integration with a
  simple paste."
- Onboarding/sign-in: "Welcome! Sign in to InSyncAI"; marketing copy "Unify
  CX across customer touchpoints."
- Example tenant in mockups: a State University (ASU Bot preview),
  showing the education use case.

TEACH FOR INDIA — DETAILS FROM CASE STUDY IMAGES
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Project metadata (from "Problem Area" slide):
- Problem: Manual processes and inconsistent messaging made it difficult for
  recruiters to maintain meaningful engagement, leading to inefficiencies,
  missed opportunities, and a fragmented candidate experience.
- Duration: 4 Weeks | Project Type: Client based | Project Category: User Journey Strategy

Highlights board:
- "Streamlined operations & a cohesive candidate journey"
- Outcome 1: Implementation Roadmap outlining a phased approach to address
  key challenges focusing on high-impact changes that improve selectivity and
  team efficiency.
- Outcome 2: Service Blueprint mapping the entire recruitment process,
  identifying friction points, and surfacing opportunities for automation and
  streamlining.
- Outcome 3: Designed Communication Templates — improving visual appeal and
  clarity while enabling quick personalization for increased candidate
  engagement and shorter response times.
- Outcome 4: Optimized Communication Strategy ensuring more consistent,
  engaging, and strategic communication across all platforms.
- Tagline: "Automated touchpoints = Quicker workflows"
- Collaborated with: Operations Team, Leadership & Strategy Team, Recruitment
  Team, Prospective Candidates, Communications Team, Fellowship Alumni.
- Impacts: 52% reduction in manual communications | 22% reduction in channels
  of communication.

Service Blueprint (titled "Teach For India Service Blueprint: Application
Process"): "A comprehensive mapping of the service provided by the Teach For
India Recruitment and Selection Teams, as Candidates navigate through the
fellowship Application Process. The map defines the Candidate journey,
Backstage Recruitment and Selection actions as well as the corresponding
communication collateral shared with Candidates as they progress through the
selection process."
- Legend — People: Selectors, Communication, Recruitment; Props & Actions
  coded as Manual / Automated / Semi-Automated (distinct line styles).
- Phases mapped: Phase 1 Fellowship preparation (Preparation); Phase 2
  Discover (Launch → Engagement → Sign-up → Registration → HM/Identification
  → Cultivation Emails); Phase 3 Cultivation/Application form.
- Swimlanes: Candidate Action, Front Stage, Back Stage, Support Processes.

Candidate Journey – Comms Evaluation matrix (DISCOVER phase): journey stages
audited — Mark attendance at event; Receive Fellowship details after Campus
event; Visit Website; Receive response to queries; Don't sign up. Timelines
audited — prior to the event; after the event (same day); 1 week after
initial email; after inviting them to an event; after Campus/Ads/Word of
Mouth; within 24 hours of sending a query; 1 week / 10 days after attending a
session; if sign up incomplete. Each touchpoint scored on: Visual Appearance,
Relevance, Content Structure, Conciseness, Tone of Voice — with documented
Pain Points and Ideas/Suggestions per cell.

Email redesign (Before → After), "Fellowship Application Submitted":
- Before: dense text email; "You'll hear from us in the next 20 working days
  on the status of your application"; links to TFI application dashboard;
  shortlist → phone interview or Assessment Centre; feedback request.
- After: visual redesign with hero photo, confirmation check icon, clearer
  hierarchy; "You'll hear from us within 10 working days about your
  application status"; Application Dashboard reminder; SHARE FEEDBACK button;
  embedded video ("Find out what's in store for you in the next two years
  from current and past applicants!" + WATCH VIDEO); sign-off "Best Wishes,
  Fellowship Selection Team, Teach For India"; contact
  admissions@teachforindia.org.
