WIX INDUSTRIAL PORTFOLIO — IMAGE-EXTRACTED PROJECT DETAILS
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

APUNAR JAPI (Graduation Project) — craft research & product design, Assam:
- "Japi is a type of hat made from bamboo and dried tokou leaves, which are
  endemic to Assam. The art of making japis is a centuries-old tradition, and
  it is considered an important cultural symbol of Assam. The handicraft is
  practiced by weaving the bamboo strips and carefully placing the tokou
  leaves to create intricate designs and patterns. Japis are used for various
  purposes, including as headgear to protect from the sun, as an accessory in
  traditional dances and ceremonies, and as decorative items in homes and
  offices. The art of japi making has been recognized as a cottage industry
  in Assam, and efforts are being made to promote and preserve this unique
  handicraft."
- Documented process: field research with japi artisans (photos of weavers
  hand-weaving bamboo), bamboo joinery experiments ("Joinery - 02" — physical
  prototypes of bamboo strip joints), and product translation: "Lighting
  mockup - 01" — a lighting fixture/lamp using japi weaving structure, with
  ideation sketches and a natural color palette (green, beige, tan, charcoal).
- Connects to the Grant Thornton artisan upskilling work and the "1,300-year-
  old craft preservation" resume bullet.

FSW-STI MANAGEMENT (Systems Design project):
- Persona storyboard ("Pooja", 8 panels): married a construction worker in
  the city → after 3 kids, husband becomes an abusive alcoholic unable to
  support the family → forced into low-income jobs where she is ill-treated
  by men → complains to a friend who offers help → friend introduces her
  first client (hotel commission) → income turns it into her everyday job →
  hides it from family by leaving at usual working hours → initially unaware
  of clients' intentions but over time learns to defend herself.
- "Journey of a Female Sex Worker" systems map — Aim: to understand the
  process a sex worker goes through from starting and entering the business
  to getting treated for an STI. Map sections: 01 Entry into Business (reason
  of joining, mode of operation), 02 Sexual Practices, Client (client
  profile: age ranges <18 to 75+, perceived economic status, disease status,
  marital status), nature of demand. Numbered steps with insights in boxes
  followed by graphic representation.
- Methodology diagram: Ideating interest areas → Ideating on negative
  emergences → Narrowing down interest area → Feasibility testing on field
  (availability of stakeholders) → Literature review (papers, videos,
  websites) → Purpose & Outcome (narrowing down to FSWs) → Primary Research
  (notes, voice recordings, photos; Day 1: FSW snowballing, Day 2: focus
  group discussion with 3 FSWs) → Data compilation (maps, mindmaps, etc.).
- Research findings: (1) working professional women differed on sacrificing
  respect vs. students willing to sacrifice respect for money; (2) all female
  student interviewees from deprived backgrounds prioritized money over
  respect, while 70% of men prioritized respect; (3) no female participants
  agreed to work with people under the influence of a substance, but 70% of
  male participants had no problem with it.
- NGO Intervention concepts: 01 Workshop kits (for NGOs educating peer
  educators — simpler, non-intimidating teaching); 1.1 Pictorial books
  (self-assistance book on steps after infection: prevention at personal
  level, whom to approach, required commodities, timeline); 1.2 Card game
  (board game where the counsellor observes choices in hypothetical
  scenarios to grasp the FSW's attitude and share information playfully);
  1.3 Interactive material (audio-visual course modules for peer educators,
  mandatory periodic completion).

GARLAND / FLOWER WEAVING TOOL (Mala project, flower vendors):
- "Personal Interviews" field research with flower vendors.
- Vendor research data table (5 samples): Sameer (27, 10 yrs experience),
  Anshul (19, 4 yrs), Ravindra (22, 6 yrs), Deepak (32, 25 yrs), Sunita
  (36, 26 yrs). Metrics captured per vendor: time to make 1m handmade garland
  (10–20 min range), selling price (₹50–70 per m), time for 1m needle-made
  garland (1–5 min), its price (₹15–40 per m), amount of sleep (0–6 hrs),
  working hours (6–22 hrs/day).

ALT — INCLUSIVE MAKE-UP BRAND:
- Title slide: "INCLUSIVE MAKE-UP BRAND. Field: Product design. Type: Team
  project. Duration: 3 weeks."
- Brand name: "alll" — cosmetic tools designed inclusively.
- User research (total sample size 19, Bhopal): Visually Impaired (NGO
  Arushi — 2 personal interviews), Hand Deformity (NGO Arushi — 6 focus
  group), Elderly (Old Age home — 1 personal interview, 3 focus group),
  Transgender Women (Mangalwara — 1 personal interview, 6 focus group).
- Product output: redesigned mascara/applicator with ergonomic teardrop
  cross-section; documented dimensions (~110 mm length, 40 mm width, 30 mm
  cap; orthographic projection scale 1:1); product renders and packaging
  (boxes, brush, tubes); photos of products with real users.

GRAY TOUCH / EDUCATIVE WORKSHOP KIT (touch & boundaries education):
- Solo testing session: Venue — Foundation Studio, National Institute of
  Design, MP; duration 30 mins; users: Shikha Agarwal, Shairly Jain, Parth
  Kothari; facilitator: Gouri Dinesh; observer: Rachit Singhi. Prototype: a
  hexagonal-tile board game with wooden character pieces and emotion/reaction
  cards.
- Child testing participants documented: Mrunal (male, 4th standard —
  talkative, smart, playful, restless, curious; more into digital games) and
  Aanya (female, 3rd standard — talkative, smart, curious, playful; more
  into educational games).
- FSW-STI/Gray Touch hero artwork: illustrated couple over a hospital
  corridor photo (Government hospital, STI/awareness context).

BAGDROP:
- Brand identity: "bagdrop" wordmark in pink with a shopping-bag-on-speed-
  lines icon (mall bag-drop service branding).

COMPASS REDESIGN (geometric compass for visually impaired students):
- Context: Secondary or higher grades | User: Visually impaired students |
  Product: Geometric Compass.
- Problem areas: existing grips are uncomfortable with minimum space and no
  ergonomic language; 500k+ students globally are completely or partially
  visually impaired; 99% — almost every visually-impaired textbook ignores
  practical chapters like geometric construction; existing compasses are too
  sharp for the visually impaired and cannot set a calculated radius; no
  braille or embossment of any type on existing products.
- Design principles ("Points to Remember"): Embossing (braille, inlays and
  outlays help users track and understand); Minimal features (too many
  features confuse students); Sharpness and form change (prevent sharpness to
  avoid accidents); Grip and texture (a comfortable grip indicates where to
  hold).

PERFUME BOTTLE (form study):
- Design direction: "Modernity, Simplicity, Elegance."
- Concept: "Maintaining a rational inspiration from the designs of Santiago
  Calatrava, combining the idea of neutral color palettes heavily used in his
  sculptures and architectural pieces... The form language inspired by the
  architect and sculptor was that of neo futurism, movement and curves
  achieved also through balance on minimal volume."
- Palette: #E2B245 (gold), #262626, #1B1B1B. Materials: transparent tinted
  glass, matt black surface. Output: black sculptural bottle render with
  curved silhouette and tinted amber window.

WIX HOMEPAGE COMMUNITY STATEMENT:
"I embrace working with various communities, especially the ones that are
unthought-of in the conventional design field. I aim at exposing myself to
new experiences whenever possible and try to learn as much as I can from
each of them."

ADDITIONAL MATHEMATICA PAGE FRAGMENTS (text on page, previously missed):
- "Who are our key users? The field workers" — reframing question asked
  mid-project before re-ideation.
- Reflection references aligning the dashboard with "StarRez" and user
  workflows (functionality-over-aesthetics learning).

Letter of recommendation Dr Sukanya Bor Saikia Associate Senior Designer, National Institute of Design (NID) It is with genuine pleasure and confidence that I recommend Mr. Rachit Singhi for admission to your Master's program in Design. Having closely known Rachit throughout his undergraduate studies (B.Des in Industrial Design from NID MP), I have observed his exceptional design aptitude, creative problem-solving skills, and commitment to innovation, which make him an outstanding candidate for advanced studies in the field of Design. Rachit was a student in several of my courses, including Interactive Design, Service Design, and Sustainable Design, where he consistently excelled, earning honors-level grades and maintaining the top rank in his class. These courses emphasized hands-on learning and real-world problem-solving, and Rachit embraced these opportunities with determination and ingenuity. One of his standout projects was an innovative luggage management service aimed at enhancing the shopping experience in malls. By identifying a key user pain point, Rachit designed a seamless system that allowed shoppers to deposit and retrieve their bags at designated points via a digital app. This solution reflected his ability to integrate technology with thoughtful service design to improve user experiences. In my Sustainable Design course, Rachit demonstrated his sensitivity to environmental challenges by addressing inefficiencies in the jute industry's manual drying processes. He designed a solar-powered dehydration system that streamlined operations, reduced manual labor, and promoted sustainability. This project underscored his ability to balance industrial scalability with ecological consciousness aligning with his passion for supporting the traditional handicraft production sector in India —an essential trait for modern designers. In another noteworthy project, Rachit designed an aid to improve the table loom weaving process, earning approval for a utility patent. By incorporating Arduino technology and crash sensors, his device automated key steps in traditional weaving while preserving its cultural essence. This project showcased his ability to merge traditional craftsmanship with modern technology, a rare and valuable skill in the design field. His innovative approach extends beyond technical solutions to include a deep empathy for users. A prime example is his development of a geometric compass for visually impaired students. This accessible tool allowed students to safely construct circles of varying radii, enabling engagement with geometry chapters often inaccessible to them. The design was not only functional but also reflective of his commitment to inclusivity in education. I was not only impressed with the design prototype but also with his empathetic approach to create tools that can help differently abled students. His contributions extend beyond academics. He has actively mentored junior students, participated in design events, and collaborated with local artisans, integrating traditional knowledge into his projects. His dedication to fostering community connections and promoting sustainable and inclusive design solutions highlights his holistic approach to the discipline. He actively sought opportunities to collaborate with local artisans, integrating their traditional knowledge into his design projects. I am confident that his passion for design, coupled with his strong academic foundation and practical experience, wil make him a valuable addition to your program. His ability to address complex challenges with innovative solutions and empathetic design aligns seamlessly with the expectations of a post graduate program. It is my sincere hope that this letter supports his application favorably. Please feel free to contact me should you require any further information. I wish him all the success for his future endeavours.
Letter of recommendation Dr. Shikha Agarwal Associate Senior Designer, National Institute of Design (NID) Rachit worked under my mentorship across several courses, including System Design, Design for Special Needs, and Design Research Methods, as well as his final graduation project. He consistently demonstrated strong proficiency, achieving the highest scores in his batch. His work reflected creativity, analytical precision, and a deep understanding of human-centric design principles. He approached every design challenge with structured ideation, prototyping, and iterative testing, ensuring practical and innovative outcomes. Rachit stood out for his clarity of thought, strong research orientation, and ability to integrate ethnographic insights into design. He was an active contributor in discussions, often offering perspectives that deepened collective understanding. His collaborative spirit, maturity, and communication skills were evident in the way he guided juniors, shared knowledge, and took initiative in department activities. During his time here, Rachit undertook projects that reflected both social awareness and creative application. He developed a mechanical tool to assist flower vendors by reducing physical strain, collaborated with local artisans on sustainable product development, explored STI management systems for marginalized communities, and designed educational tools addressing emotional well-being. His research work on the psychological effects of touch led to a co-authored paper titled “A Mixed Method Approach to Study the Effect of Touch on Self-Identity,” selected for presentation at ICoRD’25. His curiosity, empathy, and disciplined approach to design make him a thoughtful, self-motivated designer with great potential for advanced study and meaningful professional contribution.

FIGMA SECTION: Final frame Insync
(44 unique strings)
Sample layer paths:
  - Document/Page 1/Final frame Insync/Frame 2147224126/Frame 2147224163/My Role
  - Document/Page 1/Final frame Insync/Frame 2147224126/Frame 2147224162/InSync.AI, a Conversational AI platform helping enterprises deploy intelligent chatbots across customer support, e-commerce, and ed
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
A modular UI system and reusable components were shared as part of the handoff, helping developers maintain visual and interaction consistency across new screens and future features, without needing to reinvent layouts.
Building for Flexibility & Scale Even with a limited MVP (Minimum Viable Product) scope, scalability was key. We built a modular UI system with reusable components and clear patterns to keep the design consistent and adaptable as the platform grew.
Challenges
Challenges & Reflection
Clarity Over Complexity Easy navigation and minimal complexity to avoid user overwhelm and disengagement.
Clear and Confident UX The goal was to make the experience self-explanatory without overwhelming users. We built guidance directly into the interface through placeholder text, microcopy, and smart defaults, reducing friction without overusing tooltips. This created a smoother, more intuitive self-serve experience.
Cognitive Load & Information Density We replaced lengthy text with info icons and used contextual examples (ghost text) to make fields self-explanatory, streamlining the experience and reducing cognitive load.
Collaboration Builds Clarity Regular check-ins with developers and stakeholders reinforced how shared understanding turns design intent into real impact.
Collaboration with InSync was tight throughout, quick feedback loops, regular check-ins, and shared ownership of the sprint kept everyone aligned from start to finish, making sure the design remained tightly aligned with business goals and technical requirements.
Comprehensive UX Documentation We provided detailed UX notes outlining the rationale behind each design decision, along with behavioral guidelines describing how elements should respond to user actions. This ensured clarity for developers and minimized ambiguity during implementation.
Cross-Functional Alignment We maintained tight communication with development teams, walking them through the prototype and component logic to ensure shared understanding of functionality, interaction patterns, and intended user experience before build-out began.
Design
Design Guidelines
Design Systems are Strategic Building the design guidelines early taught me that systems drive scalability and team alignment, not just visual consistency.
Development
Discovery
Final design screens
Grasping every stage of the chatbot creation process and its purpose was initially tough, it required close collaboration to map how everything fit together.
Handoff
Highlights
I had to build a modular system that served the MVP now but could expand later, without overcomplicating the first release.
I was responsible for understanding the different stages and features that needed to be designed as part of the complete user flow, and for creating the final product from scratch based on the defined requirements. From translating initial wireframes into a cohesive visual system to refining interactions for clarity and scalability, I led the end-to-end design process. I also managed the developer handoff, documenting design logic, interaction behavior, and UI guidelines to ensure accurate implementation and consistency across the product.
Ideation
InSync.AI brought a mindset of testing fast, releasing early, and iterating with intention, something that has since shaped our own approach to design sprints. Instead of chasing pixel-perfect from day one, they encouraged progress over perfection: ship a strong foundation, gather feedback, and evolve rapidly. It was a healthy reminder that the best products aren’t static, they’re living systems that get smarter over time.
InSync.AI, a Conversational AI platform helping enterprises deploy intelligent chatbots across customer support, e-commerce, and education, partnered with us to design a no-code configuration platform that enables users to create and customize chatbots effortlessly. The goal was to simplify a traditionally technical process, allowing teams to configure chatbot behavior, tone, and integrations through an intuitive interface. Designed for both first-time users and enterprise power users, the platform streamlines chatbot creation, reduces development dependency, and accelerates deployment across use cases.
My Role
Navigation Flexibility We replaced the top progress bar with a left-side navigation panel, allowing users to revisit or skip sections easily , improving flexibility and perceived control.
Navigation That Mirrors Real-World Use We organized the interface around real user journeys, grouping related tasks to mirror how customer success teams onboard and support clients. This made setup more intuitive and helped new users navigate without confusion.
Project Overview
Reflections and Learnings
SaaS Meets Simplicity A fusion of industrial SaaS functionality and a modern, minimalistic aesthetic, despite the product being a backend solution.
Scalability & Visual Hierarchy We restructured the layout into a modular, scalable framework with clear visual hierarchy, ensuring that new chatbot capabilities could be added seamlessly later.
Secondary Research
Semantic Intuition Interfaces should feel familiar and let users make decisions quickly.
Several parameters were considered during the handoff
Small Interactions Matter Subtle details like ghost text and info icons proved how microcopy and hierarchy can make complex workflows feel effortless.
Testing
Through collaborative ideation sessions with the product and development teams, I refined concepts for how users could configure chatbots in a guided, step-by-step manner.
Timeline
Turning technical chatbot configurations into an intuitive, guided experience took multiple iterations and tight feedback loops.
We began by immersing ourselves in the product space, aligning with InSync’s business goals, benchmarking similar tools, and identifying UX patterns that worked well (and didn’t). This helped us define the principles our design needed to uphold:
We explored multiple layout options for each feature to identify the most intuitive and efficient configuration flow, balancing usability with visual clarity.
Working closely with the Insync team, we mapped the product’s core user flows and structured the sprint around clusters of related features, enabling rapid prototyping while ensuring cohesion.
Working with a team so invested in feedback loops and continuous improvement made this project not just a design exercise, but a genuinely collaborative build. As of 2025, the product is in active development, with future iterations rolling out based on learnings from early users, built on a flexible system we crafted together.

Brand Guidelines
Challenges
Challenges & Reflection
Clear documentation of the UX ensured consistency in future iterations and technical scalability. While the modular layout allowed seamless data integration and expansions without the need for a complete redesign.
Collaborative cooperation Working with diverse stakeholders across geographies strengthened the ability to translate varied goals into one coherent, human-centered solution.
Comparisons were key Leadership wanted to track progress across different countries, crops, and other variables.
Customizability The ability to customize dashboards to fit specific needs allows for a more personalized experience.

Ideation
Mathematica, a leader in evidence-based research and data analysis, partnered with AGRA, a non-profit driving agricultural transformation in Africa, to create a data dashboard for tracking progress across countries. They asked us to design this dashboard, transforming complex, multi-source datasets into a clear, intuitive tool for decision-making. AGRA leaders, from country directors to VPs, would use it to assess progress, identify gaps, and compare results by country and crop during quarterly and annual planning.
Micro-visualizations Small, glanceable charts allowed users to see trends quickly, while interactive filters and drill-downs let them explore further.
My Role
Our design approach focused on creating a dashboard that was both scalable and intuitive, ensuring users could navigate data effortlessly while accommodating future growth. Here’s how we made it happen:
Our early explorations focused on finding the right structure and visual language to balance detail with clarity. We mapped workflows, tested layout variations, and drafted interaction models that would support both strategic overviews and deeper analysis. Visualizations required interactivity—filters, drill-downs, and hover-to-reveal tooltips—allowing users to customize views for deeper exploration and understanding.
Project Overview
Reflections and Learnings
Researching effective data visualization methods Analyzed best practices and existing dashboards across sectors to determine suitable visualization types for agricultural indicators, ensuring clarity, comparability, and ease of interpretation.
Scalability was another key focus. The design was built to support future growth through modular layouts and a defined design system, allowing the platform to evolve even in the absence of a dedicated designer.
Secondary Research
Simplicity was crucial Users needed a dashboard that provided at-a-glance insights without overwhelming them with data.
Specific drill downs Additional filters and comparison tools would allow users to drill down into specific data points.
Testing
The first major challenge was balancing diverse stakeholder needs.
The main challenge lay in AGRA’s complex, multi-variable dataset spanning regions and time periods. Before designing, we conducted stakeholder interviews to understand their needs, challenges, and expectations for how the dashboard should support their workflow.
The project also involved cross-continental collaboration between teams in the United States, Africa, and India, which demanded extensive coordination across time zones, languages, and workflows.
The solution turned a complex data challenge into a visually engaging, user-friendly tool. A high-level summary on the landing page highlighted key goals for instant comprehension, while micro-visualizations offered snapshot insights with hover or click details. The final choices ensured consistency in color, navigation, and visual hierarchy for a seamless experience. Each visualization was assessed for clarity, comparisons across regions and time periods, and quick trend detection, using tailored approaches like grouped bar and line graphs.
Timeline
Tradeoffs Clarity vs visual appeal Some visualizations were visually engaging but difficult for leadership teams to interpret quickly. Simplicity was prioritized over visual complexity. Scalability vs detail Certain graph types worked well initially but became harder to maintain and compare as the dataset grew. Consistency vs experimentation The introduction of micro visualizations created a visual disconnect from the more detailed charts, requiring greater consistency across the dashboard. Our design approach focused on creating a dashboard that was both scalable and intuitive, ensuring users could navigate data effortlessly while accommodating future growth. Here’s how we made it happen:
Understanding R Shiny’s capabilities Explored R Shiny’s potential for building interactive, data-driven dashboards suited to AGRA’s scale. Reviewed case studies and documentation to identify how its visualization tools could handle complex datasets effectively.

Challenges & Reflection

Community & Mutual Aid The lander desk isn’t just a package retrieval desk; it acts like a node of the campus for student support. Every shift that I have had involved facilitating a direct exchange between the university’s logistics and students who needed something, whether it's help with the retrieval of a package, a textbook, medication, or a care package from home. What makes this mutual aid rather than just a transactional is the human layer involved, verification of identity, locating the right package goes to the right person, and catching any problems that occur. The desk representative bridges that gap between an automated system and a person standing to help them, which requires communication and judgement which automated system wouldn’t be able to do on its own.

Consequences Could lead to faster and a lot more confident verification with very little and only restructuring data costs.
Consequences It reduces inconsistency and reliance on other employees for help when such issues arise and won't slow down during edge-case handling, especially during rush hours. Problem Diagnosis
Problem Identification verification relies on multiple data points that are inconsistently communicated, creating friction and moments of doubt in the process.
Problem The entire system supports the transactional steps to a certain extent but completely ignores the communicative ones, leaving employees to self-develop and figure out the social interaction scripts that the workflow doesn’t always account for.
Problem The system assumes everything will go right, and when it doesn’t, there is no guidance to fall back on, leaving employees to self-develop hacks on the spot.

Signing Pad
Solution A built-in FAQ system that acts as a fallback for employees, especially when no leads or supervisors are available to solve the problem. Like an FAQ system on how to check past scanned shelves or an easier indication to shift packages to the claimed section, which would formalize the instructions for new employees, especially.
Solution Consolidating the identity signals in the digital system, like displaying legal name, preferred name, room number, and dorm together in one clear view, which would reduce the cognitive load on the employee.
Solution Some large and overarching suggested phrasing for communicative friction points, like when failing to catch names or mismatches between the system, would standardize what is currently inconsistent across employees. Additionally, something a lot more speculative or futuristic could be a system that could translate the spoken word into speech for accent interpretations.

The initial goal was to improve communication, but a deeper issue emerged: the absence of a unified system. Messaging was inconsistent, visually fragmented, and manually intensive across teams. The Service Blueprint
The system displays any available packages on the employee-facing screen Touch Point - The digital software
The system seems linear in nature, with a step-by-step process, but no guidance for when those steps break down, especially because it's heavily reliant on a lot of variables with no acknowledgment of those breakdowns. For example, when there are a lot of packages, a barcode is damaged, or a package is missing, I improvise a solution in real-time, like photographing the location list, seeking help from a lead, or adjusting myself physically due to the unergonomic placement of the scanner. None of these workarounds are accounted for and documented, and one needs to develop them with experience.
Theme Selected
There are multiple times in the process where a student's identity needs to be verified, which is layered. The husky card, scan, last name, and room number. Each of these layers exists to reveal something that the previous one didn’t and make sure that the package is meant for the student who has come to pick it up. But each of these layers reveals a friction point, like accent challenges, mismatched names, and missing room numbers, meaning that the verification process is simultaneously the most important and most error-prone part of the interaction.

To complement the blueprint, we mapped communication hotspots across the ecosystem. It exposed duplication, like multiple email IDs, and highlighted opportunities to consolidate channels, ease management, and reduce team fatigue.

User Interviews

We also analyzed recruitment data, application highs and lows, drop-off points, and candidate feedback, to get a pulse on the system’s performance, giving us a quantitative perspective of where the experience could be improved.
We began with a two-day Wisdom Mining Workshop, a deep dive into the entire recruitment journey. By bringing together all the teams a candidate would interact with, we created space for cross-functional understanding, helping everyone see the process from different perspectives. This broke down silos, uncovered friction points, and surfaced shared challenges.
We conducted a Training Workshop with the entire team, equipping them with the tools to read and refine the service blueprint, adjust communication strategies as needed, and manage future challenges independently. The three activities revolved around this ownership.
We created a detailed service blueprint outlining every stage and substage of the candidate journey. It captured all touch points, front-stage and backstage actions, supporting processes, and collateral at each step. The review uncovered that 40% of routine touch points, intros, reminders, follow-ups, could be automated, saving effort while keeping engagement high.
We mapped manual, automated, and semi-automated interactions with distinct line styles. Color-coded props and people helped trace communication flows, highlight overlaps, and uncover inefficiencies across the journey. The index and an example of use below.
We redesigned key emails for Gmail templates and refined them through RITE (Rapid Iterative Testing Exercise) testing with real candidates. Evaluation revealed that even templating just 10% of the materials improved efficiency and consistency, without losing personalization. An example of a design created is below with the comparison.
What happens between scanning your card and getting your package?
What started as a messaging redesign became a structural transformation. The findings indicated 8 root challenges, ranging from timing misalignment to redundant workflows. Using a prioritization matrix, we created a phased roadmap, focusing first on the changes with the biggest impact.
Whe a student knows the process because of repeated interactions, the whole interaction flows; they offer their information upfront, wait patiently, and the handoff is smooth.
When a barcode is damaged or awkwardly placed, multiple scan attempts fail, and I again mark it as claimed manually, sometimes using the tracking ID printed on the label as a fallback.
When a package is missing from its indicated shelf, I reach out to the lead on duty. There's no training or step-by-step guide for this situation, even though the system does have a way to view all shelves used during the same time period.
When a student has multiple packages, I look for a Post-it and pen to write down the shelf locations, but they're usually not at the desk. When I can't find them, I take a photo of the location list on my phone instead.
When a student has multiple packages, they're often on different shelves, requiring multiple trips. I have no easy way to hold all the locations in my head at once.
When a student skips the greeting and jumps straight to "I have a package," I adapt mid-sentence and move directly into the flow.
When a student's room number doesn't match the label, I ask if they recently changed rooms, which usually resolves the mismatch.
When no room number is on the label, I verify the dorm name instead as a backup check.
When students look unsure about where to scan, I use a verbal prompt or gesture toward the scanner to guide them.
When the name on the system doesn't match what the student says, it's often because the system displays both their legal and preferred names in separate places, causing a moment of confusion during identity verification.
When there's a queue forming, I shift into a faster, more clipped version of my usual script to keep things moving.
When there's no space on the counter, and I set a package down in front of the student, they sometimes assume they can just take it before I've had the chance to scan the barcode. In that case, I manually update the system to mark it as claimed.

10 Weeks
80°
A user survey was circulated across the UW community to obtain quantitative data on 21 UW students who have experienced the rainy season in Seattle. The survey overviewed the students’ commute schedule, the rain gear they bought and used, and common pain points when navigating through rainy days. Building on the insights from the user surveys, we also conducted 5 semi-structured user interviews with UW students who regularly use public transport and commute to campus. The interviews allowed for deeper storytelling and highlighted key infrastructural issues in accessibility, mobility, and safety.
Accounting for Scalability
After sketching design ideas individually, our team conducted an affinity-mapping exercise to organize our concepts into four categories: stand-alone overhangs, attached overhangs, drying/storage facilities, and rain gear rental systems.
Application Feedback 01. Confusing labelling/terminology use in the application. I 02. Lacks appropriate feedback provision. I 03. Labelling the overhang better for better communication. I 04. Suggested consideration of all use-cases (How would we show a broken overhang, multi request handing) II 05. Include features of resolved issues I
Automation
Balancing prototype fidelity limitations—1:15 scale model couldn't demonstrate wind resistance or mechanical stress, requiring reliance on hypothetical scenarios.
Based on our user research, over 70% of our participants expressed frustrations with the lack of shelter and overhang infrastructures on campus. We decided to focus on a design solution that directly addressed this pain point and offered a more accessible and feasible way for students to stay dry on campus. By integrating with existing campus structures, this solution would minimize installation barriers and fit cohesively with the current environment at UW.
Because the need to design a companion app for maintenance was identified much later in the design process, the time constraint left us unable to conduct formal user research on the UW Maintenance Staff. As a result, many of the design decisions for the app are based on engineering requirements to operate the overhangs and assumptions about logistics . Further research would need to be conducted to understand the staff’s best practices and design the most intuitive flows.

Reflections
Research Timing and Depth: We learned that the sequencing of research matters as much as its content, front-loading contextual inquiry and behavioral research enables faster iteration, preventing late-stage redesigns.
Safety Concerns in the Rain Many students shared how specific areas on campus were slippery or poorly lit, raising accessibility and safety concerns.
Scheduled Service
Seattle gets rain on roughly half the days of the year, making wet weather a constant part of life, yet quality rain gear—ranging from about $70 to over $400—can be a financial burden for newcomers, especially students from drier regions. Our design question asks how to shift the responsibility for staying dry from individuals to institutions, and how the University of Washington can offer more accessible, affordable rain-gear options for its students.

Stakeholder Inclusion: Omitting UW facilities staff from early research led to inaccurate workflow assumptions in the maintenance app, teaching us that every stakeholder, especially operational users, must be directly involved to build effective, multi-sided solutions.

The awning arm that allows the retractibility
The cover in which the overhang rolls into
The Drizzle App is a companion app for UW Maintenance Staff to monitor the overall wellbeing of the overhang system around campus. Its integrated weather API provides weather forecast data that allows for the automation of the overhang system above a rain probability threshold. Core flows include manual open/close, immediate service dispatch, scheduling service, and notification filters.
The Drizzle Companion App
The Drizzle Modular Overhang
The Drizzle Modular Overhang envisions a retractable rain-shield system that seamlessly integrates into existing vertical poles. This can include bus stop poles, light poles, or traffic poles across UW campus. In large open spaces, like Red Square, a collapsible pole can be installed to support the overhang.
The Drizzle Project addresses this issue by introducing an integrated system of modular overhangs installed and maintained by the institution. This solution enables students who frequently commute and walk around campus to stay dry without relying on expensive rain gear or carrying heavy rain equipment. Additionally, this solution provides an accessible option for students with mobility challenges, offering sheltered places to rest and eliminating the need to manage additional rain gear.
The Drizzle Project introduces two key components: The Drizzle Modular Overhang and The Drizzle App used by UW staff to maintain these systems. The Drizzle Project offers an accessible, safe, and convenient way for students to stay dry on campus.
The Drizzle Project Navigating Seattle Rain Without Gear
The Drizzle Project tackles this issue with modular, institution-maintained overhangs that keep commuting students dry without costly or cumbersome rain gear. These shelters also support students with mobility challenges by offering accessible, covered places to rest without managing extra equipment.
The final design measurements were based on the 115.25" bus pole height, allowing 36" overhangs without obstructing height clearance. Each side stands 42" tall, accommodating about 5–6 people, and these proportions were validated through 3D simulation using real dimensions. (Measurements in inches)
The Final Product
The overhead
The QR code activation method created significant friction, with 4 out of 5 participants preferring either physical buttons or automated deployment. Users demonstrated a one-minute patience threshold before abandoning closed overhangs, indicating that our staff-mediated approval process would fail in real-world conditions.
The service blueprint summarizes the end-to-end experience of The Drizzle Project. It illustrates how students interact with the modular overhang system on campus and how the UW Maintenance Staff and backend systems support those interactions. By visualizing multiple user flows that integrate, the blueprint clarifies how the system works together.
Time Constraints
Timeline
To being getting a better understanding of what the overhang would look like in context, we created a Wizard-of-Oz prototype, building the overhangs and prototyping core user flows for a companion app for UW Maintenance Staff. This prototype was primarily to demonstrate how the staff would manually activate the overhang upon request, mostly to counter instances of misuse that might occur. This process helped us refine details of the overhang’s mechanical systems and adjust its measurements, in addition to begin scaffolding a structure for the companion app.
Two user personas were developed to represent UW students that walk around campus and those who commute via public transportation. These personas highlighted key pain points, such as financial constraints and accessibility barriers, which informed and guided our design priorities.
two-sided clamp for better strength
Understanding Seattle’s Rain Culture Identified cultural norms, social stigmas, and precedents established by those who have lived and experienced Seattle’s rainy season. This established a contextual understanding of our problem space.

UX Case Study
Validating physical infrastructure within academic timeline constraints without access to full-scale prototyping or weather simulation capabilities.
We conducted usability testing with five participants to evaluate the 1:15 scale prototype and maintenance app interface. Each 15-20 minute session involved scenario-based tasks that revealed how users interacted with the QR code activation system, assessed the overhang's perceived effectiveness, and identified friction points in the staff workflow. We analyzed feedback using an intensity-vs-scope framework, plotting each issue by severity and participant frequency to prioritize design revisions systematically.
When the overhang system starts scaling in size across campus, the Notifications Modal would become difficult to navigate due to the sheer volume of requests that would come in. Users suggested features like notification filters to help account for this volume over time.

A new calender would be created where you can share common requirments with the users of the calender.
Adding features to an existing app which already as features similar to whats required would solve the purpose and save the users time in installing a new application all together.
High fidelity mock-up
High Fidelity Wireframe
Search and enter requirments along with the quantity required to be purchased.
Select the accounts that you would want to share your calender with.
The immediate requirments would allow one to notify someone specific sending them a notificationon their phones.
The plus icon available in google calender could have an additional feature of shared calenders.
The user could have multiple shared calenders and create new ones, synced from google contacts too.
The user would get a notification once the request has been sent to the one using the calender.
The users would have 2 main features as soon as they tap on a date. A checklist - that allows them to store all the requirments on a particular date. Immediate reuirments - List immediate reuirments from on the app that would notify the user of your desire to purchase avoinding miscommunication.

Insight Designing a single, intuitive dashboard helped bridge silos and enabled cross-country visibility. It allowed AGRA’s leadership to track KPIs in real time, spot problem areas faster, and make more coordinated, data-driven decisions.

Insight The success of the dashboard depended less on aesthetics and more on how seamlessly it functioned within AGRA’s existing systems and workflows. We tested various graph and visualization types to determine which best supported AGRA’s leadership in understanding the data. FIGMA SECTION: Frame 2147224219
(1 unique strings)

For leadership to gain real value from the dashboard, the data had to be more than just visually appealing but functionally effective. We explored different graph and visualization types to identify those that best supported clarity, comparison, and trend detection. Interactivity was key—features like filters, drill-downs, and hover-to-reveal tooltips allowed users to focus on specific metrics and explore data intuitively. The final visual system maintained consistency in color, navigation, and hierarchy, ensuring a cohesive and seamless experience.

FIGMA SECTION: Group 1000004294

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Collaborated with
Insight Designing a single, intuitive dashboard helped bridge silos and enabled cross-country visibility. It allowed AGRA’s leadership to track KPIs in real time, spot problem areas faster, and make more coordinated, data-driven decisions.
Insight Interviews revealed that decision-makers weren’t struggling with lack of information but with overwhelming, unstructured data. They needed a concise, visual way to compare performance across countries and metrics — something that turned data into direction.
Insight The success of the dashboard depended less on aesthetics and more on how seamlessly it functioned within AGRA’s existing systems and workflows. Ensuring compatibility with tools like StarRez and meeting operational requirements became central to the design process, proving that usability and integration mattered more than visual polish.
Single platform for all data tracking requirements

A modular UI system and reusable components were shared as part of the handoff, helping developers maintain visual and interaction consistency across new screens and future features, without needing to reinvent layouts.
Activity 1 – Service Blueprint Mapping Solved a blueprint puzzle to understand the recruitment communication flow.
Activity 2 – Professional Email Writing Designing Email and WhatsApp invites for candidate recruitment using provided templates.
Activity 3 – Communication Collateral Design Matched collaterals to the correct service stages to understand effective message timing.
Balancing structure with empathy Standardizing communication without losing its personal, human tone required constant refinement.
Brand Guidelines
Building for Flexibility & Scale Even with a limited MVP (Minimum Viable Product) scope, scalability was key. We built a modular UI system with reusable components and clear patterns to keep the design consistent and adaptable as the platform grew.
By addressing both strategic and structural gaps, we not only enhanced the candidate experience but also added a scalable system that could grow with TFI’s evolving recruitment needs.
Candidates from diverse backgrounds, college students and working professionals, responded differently to outreach, revealing the need for tailored communication strategies.
Challenges
Challenges & Reflection
Clarity Over Complexity Easy navigation and minimal complexity to avoid user overwhelm and disengagement.
Clear and Confident UX The goal was to make the experience self-explanatory without overwhelming users. We built guidance directly into the interface through placeholder text, microcopy, and smart defaults, reducing friction without overusing tooltips. This created a smoother, more intuitive self-serve experience.
Clear documentation of the UX ensured consistency in future iterations and technical scalability. While the modular layout allowed seamless data integration and expansions without the need for a complete redesign.
Cognitive Load & Information Density We replaced lengthy text with info icons and used contextual examples (ghost text) to make fields self-explanatory, streamlining the experience and reducing cognitive load.
Collaborating across stakeholders Aligning diverse teams and perspectives pushed me to communicate clearly and think systemically.
Collaboration Builds Clarity Regular check-ins with developers and stakeholders reinforced how shared understanding turns design intent into real impact.
Collaboration with InSync was tight throughout, quick feedback loops, regular check-ins, and shared ownership of the sprint kept everyone aligned from start to finish, making sure the design remained tightly aligned with business goals and technical requirements.
Collaborative cooperation Working with diverse stakeholders across geographies strengthened the ability to translate varied goals into one coherent, human-centered solution.
Comfort in Uncertainty Early stages were messy, many directions, incomplete data, and shifting insights. Embracing that ambiguity taught me to stay open, experiment fast, and trust the iterative process.
Comparisons were key Leadership wanted to track progress across different countries, crops, and other variables.
Comprehensive UX Documentation We provided detailed UX notes outlining the rationale behind each design decision, along with behavioral guidelines describing how elements should respond to user actions. This ensured clarity for developers and minimized ambiguity during implementation.
Cross-Functional Alignment We maintained tight communication with development teams, walking them through the prototype and component logic to ensure shared understanding of functionality, interaction patterns, and intended user experience before build-out began.
Customizability The ability to customize dashboards to fit specific needs allows for a more personalized experience.
Customization mattered Different teams had different needs, so the dashboard had to allow users to tailor their views.
Design
Design Guidelines
Design Systems are Strategic Building the design guidelines early taught me that systems drive scalability and team alignment, not just visual consistency.
Designing for clarity Simplifying complex, multi-country datasets into clear, actionable visuals showed how design can directly influence better, faster decision-making.
Designing for Scale and Flexibility
Despite the differences, we worked together to refine the dashboard through structured feedback loops, iterative design sprints, and continuous validation with end-users. Design, in many ways, became the universal language that bridged all differences.
Development
Discovery
Disjointed Social Media Limits Impact Spontaneous content and an inconsistent visual identity hurt engagement. Without a unified narrative, even strong posts lacked recall and failed to convert across audience segments.
Each use case scenario was also accounted for—covering no-data, zero states, active states, and all other possible user interactions—to make the system resilient and reliable.
Ensuring compatibility with R Shiny, the template-based development tool used by the organization, was critical. Every layout, interaction, and feature was designed with its technical limitations and capabilities in mind.
Final design screens
Finally, technical limitations within the development software required careful trade-offs between design intent and feasible implementation.
Flexible Layout A modular, scalable framework accommodating future data additions without a full redesign.
From Communication to Structural Transformation
Functionality over aesthetics The project reinforced that true value lies in how well a system works, not just how it looks. Aligning the dashboard with StarRez and user workflows proved far more impactful than visual embellishments.
Grasping every stage of the chatbot creation process and its purpose was initially tough, it required close collaboration to map how everything fit together.
Handoff
Handoff & Workshop
Highlights
Hits, Misses and Things to Start Doing Participants evaluated each recruitment stage to identify what was working well, what created friction, and what new practices could enhance engagement and efficiency. This structured reflection helped prioritize focus areas for redesign and revealed opportunities for automation and more personalized outreach.
I had to build a modular system that served the MVP now but could expand later, without overcomplicating the first release.
I joined the project as a designer, responsible for developing the user flow and mapping the end-to-end candidate journey through the Service Blueprint. While the initial mapping framework was in place, I led the ideation and refinement of the blueprinting approach, its development, and the subsequent templatization of key communication collaterals. I also contributed to data analysis and insight synthesis alongside the project lead, while independently executing all graphic design work. This was a highly collaborative project, with clearly defined roles and strong cross-team coordination throughout.
I was involved from inception to completion, leading efforts to organize and interpret scattered data, explore effective visualization approaches, and manage testing, refinement, and final handover. While collaborating closely with my team for feedback and insights, I developed a strong sense of ownership and deepened my understanding of data visualization, both technically and strategically.
I was responsible for understanding the different stages and features that needed to be designed as part of the complete user flow, and for creating the final product from scratch based on the defined requirements. From translating initial wireframes into a cohesive visual system to refining interactions for clarity and scalability, I led the end-to-end design process. I also managed the developer handoff, documenting design logic, interaction behavior, and UI guidelines to ensure accurate implementation and consistency across the product.
Ideation
Inconsistency Weakens Trust and Connection Across emails, WhatsApp, and social media, shifting tone and voice diluted TFI’s mission and confused candidates. The lack of emotional coherence made the brand feel disjointed and eroded confidence in the process.
InSync.AI brought a mindset of testing fast, releasing early, and iterating with intention, something that has since shaped our own approach to design sprints. Instead of chasing pixel-perfect from day one, they encouraged progress over perfection: ship a strong foundation, gather feedback, and evolve rapidly. It was a healthy reminder that the best products aren’t static, they’re living systems that get smarter over time.
InSync.AI, a Conversational AI platform helping enterprises deploy intelligent chatbots across customer support, e-commerce, and education, partnered with us to design a no-code configuration platform that enables users to create and customize chatbots effortlessly. The goal was to simplify a traditionally technical process, allowing teams to configure chatbot behavior, tone, and integrations through an intuitive interface. Designed for both first-time users and enterprise power users, the platform streamlines chatbot creation, reduces development dependency, and accelerates deployment across use cases.
Introductions We began by bringing together all recruitment teams to share their roles and interactions with candidates, building empathy across functions and setting a common understanding of the process. This session helped surface interdependencies early on, breaking silos and preparing the ground for collaborative problem-solving.
Key Learnings
Manual Tasks Strain Team Capacity Repetitive communication, like reminders and follow ups, led to fatigue and errors. Automating just 40% of these interactions could dramatically boost team productivity and focus.
Mathematica, a leader in evidence-based research and data analysis, partnered with AGRA, a non-profit driving agricultural transformation in Africa, to create a data dashboard for tracking progress across countries. They asked us to design this dashboard, transforming complex, multi-source datasets into a clear, intuitive tool for decision-making. AGRA leaders, from country directors to VPs, would use it to assess progress, identify gaps, and compare results by country and crop during quarterly and annual planning.
Micro-visualizations Small, glanceable charts allowed users to see trends quickly, while interactive filters and drill-downs let them explore further.
My Role
Navigation Flexibility We replaced the top progress bar with a left-side navigation panel, allowing users to revisit or skip sections easily , improving flexibility and perceived control.
Navigation That Mirrors Real-World Use We organized the interface around real user journeys, grouping related tasks to mirror how customer success teams onboard and support clients. This made setup more intuitive and helped new users navigate without confusion.
Open-Ended Problem Statement Teams collaboratively reframed broad challenges into open-ended questions, encouraging deeper reflection on what effective, human-centered communication could look like. This exercise aligned everyone around the core purpose, ensuring recruitment communication not only informed but inspired potential Fellows.
Organizing scattered data Bringing together hundreds of communication pieces across platforms into one coherent system was complex and time-intensive.
Our design approach focused on creating a dashboard that was both scalable and intuitive, ensuring users could navigate data effortlessly while accommodating future growth. Here’s how we made it happen:
Our early explorations focused on finding the right structure and visual language to balance detail with clarity. We mapped workflows, tested layout variations, and drafted interaction models that would support both strategic overviews and deeper analysis. Visualizations required interactivity—filters, drill-downs, and hover-to-reveal tooltips—allowing users to customize views for deeper exploration and understanding.
People Over Processes This project showed that communication design is about how information feels, not just consistency, understanding the candidate’s emotional journey made the design more empathetic and impactful.
Personas We developed two representative personas, Naina, a recent graduate exploring meaningful career options, and Varun, a working professional seeking impact, to anchor insights in real candidate motivations. These personas helped teams empathize with different applicant mindsets, guiding the tone, timing, and medium of future communication.
Problem Diagnosis
Project Overview
Reflections and Learnings
Researching effective data visualization methods Analyzed best practices and existing dashboards across sectors to determine suitable visualization types for agricultural indicators, ensuring clarity, comparability, and ease of interpretation.
SaaS Meets Simplicity A fusion of industrial SaaS functionality and a modern, minimalistic aesthetic, despite the product being a backend solution.
Scalability & Visual Hierarchy We restructured the layout into a modular, scalable framework with clear visual hierarchy, ensuring that new chatbot capabilities could be added seamlessly later.
Scalability was another key focus. The design was built to support future growth through modular layouts and a defined design system, allowing the platform to evolve even in the absence of a dedicated designer.
Scattered Ownership Causes Confusion No single owner of communication meant fragmented experiences and delays. Centralized guidelines and accountability improved clarity, consistency, and candidate experience.
Secondary Research
Semantic Intuition Interfaces should feel familiar and let users make decisions quickly.
Several parameters were considered during the handoff
Simplicity was crucial Users needed a dashboard that provided at-a-glance insights without overwhelming them with data.
Small Interactions Matter Subtle details like ghost text and info icons proved how microcopy and hierarchy can make complex workflows feel effortless.
Specific drill downs Additional filters and comparison tools would allow users to drill down into specific data points.
Structured Collaboration I learned that large, complex projects move forward only when everyone feels heard and understood. Building shared ownership through workshops and visual tools like blueprints created alignment far faster than endless discussions could.
Teach For India, a non-profit working to end educational inequity through its nationwide Fellowship program, partnered with us to streamline and enhance its recruitment communications. The goal was to simplify a complex, largely manual process, creating a unified, efficient, and engaging communication system that delivers the right message at the right time. Designed to balance automation with personalization, the solution reduces recruiter workload, ensures consistency across channels, and strengthens the overall candidate experience.
Teams recognized the value of unifying messaging across platforms to build a consistent, inspiring narrative that reflects TFI’s mission and strengthens candidate engagement.
Testing
The Ecosystem Map
The first major challenge was balancing diverse stakeholder needs.
The initial goal was to improve communication, but a deeper issue emerged: the absence of a unified system. Messaging was inconsistent, visually fragmented, and manually intensive across teams. We audited 200+ content pieces, emails to social posts, using five criteria (visuals, relevance, structure, conciseness, tone) to find alignment gaps and built a scoring matrix with clear benchmarks for each.
The main challenge lay in AGRA’s complex, multi-variable dataset spanning regions and time periods. Before designing, we conducted stakeholder interviews to understand their needs, challenges, and expectations for how the dashboard should support their workflow.
The one-on-one conversations across teams and candidates, gave us a clear view of both strategic priorities and on-ground realities. Together, this mix of structured collaboration and candid dialogue helped us get to the heart of the problem. The key problems revealed were -
The project also involved cross-continental collaboration between teams in the United States, Africa, and India, which demanded extensive coordination across time zones, languages, and workflows.
The Service Blueprint
The solution turned a complex data challenge into a visually engaging, user-friendly tool. A high-level summary on the landing page highlighted key goals for instant comprehension, while micro-visualizations offered snapshot insights with hover or click details. The final choices ensured consistency in color, navigation, and visual hierarchy for a seamless experience. Each visualization was assessed for clarity, comparisons across regions and time periods, and quick trend detection, using tailored approaches like grouped bar and line graphs.
Through collaborative ideation sessions with the product and development teams, I refined concepts for how users could configure chatbots in a guided, step-by-step manner.
Timeline
To complement the blueprint, we mapped communication hotspots across the ecosystem. It exposed duplication, like multiple email IDs, and highlighted opportunities to consolidate channels, ease management, and reduce team fatigue.
Turning technical chatbot configurations into an intuitive, guided experience took multiple iterations and tight feedback loops.
Uncovering the Root Problem
Understanding R Shiny’s capabilities Explored R Shiny’s potential for building interactive, data-driven dashboards suited to AGRA’s scale. Reviewed case studies and documentation to identify how its visualization tools could handle complex datasets effectively.
User Interviews
User research
User-Friendly Navigation Users could navigate seamlessly while always knowing their place within the dashboard.
UX Guidelines
Variations in tone and quality across channels, especially on social media, created a fragmented brand experience and diluted TFI’s overall presence.
We also analyzed recruitment data, application highs and lows, drop-off points, and candidate feedback, to get a pulse on the system’s performance, giving us a quantitative perspective of where the experience could be improved.
We began by immersing ourselves in the product space, aligning with InSync’s business goals, benchmarking similar tools, and identifying UX patterns that worked well (and didn’t). This helped us define the principles our design needed to uphold:
We began with a two-day Wisdom Mining Workshop, a deep dive into the entire recruitment journey. By bringing together all the teams a candidate would interact with, we created space for cross-functional understanding, helping everyone see the process from different perspectives. This broke down silos, uncovered friction points, and surfaced shared challenges.
We conducted a Training Workshop with the entire team, equipping them with the tools to read and refine the service blueprint, adjust communication strategies as needed, and manage future challenges independently. The three activities revolved around this ownership.
We created a detailed service blueprint outlining every stage and substage of the candidate journey. It captured all touch points, front-stage and backstage actions, supporting processes, and collateral at each step. The review uncovered that 40% of routine touch points, intros, reminders, follow-ups, could be automated, saving effort while keeping engagement high.
We explored multiple layout options for each feature to identify the most intuitive and efficient configuration flow, balancing usability with visual clarity.
We mapped manual, automated, and semi-automated interactions with distinct line styles. Color-coded props and people helped trace communication flows, highlight overlaps, and uncover inefficiencies across the journey. The index and an example of use below.
We redesigned key emails for Gmail templates and refined them through RITE (Rapid Iterative Testing Exercise) testing with real candidates. Evaluation revealed that even templating just 10% of the materials improved efficiency and consistency, without losing personalization. An example of a design created is below with the comparison.
We tested various graph and visualization types to determine which best supported AGRA’s leadership in understanding the data. Customized user flows and journeys were developed for each testing group, including country heads, business line leaders, and senior management, to ensure the experience aligned with their specific roles and decision-making needs. This process revealed opportunities for improvement, including:
We worked closely with the development team to make sure our designs were not just visually effective but also feasible within the technical constraints. We iterated quickly, testing and refining components to strike the perfect balance between aesthetics, functionality, and performance.
We worked closely with the development team to make sure our designs were not just visually effective but also feasible within the technical constraints. We iterated quickly, testing and refining components to strike the perfect balance between aesthetics, functionality, and performance. Several parameters were considered during the handoff
What started as a messaging redesign became a structural transformation. The findings indicated 8 root challenges, ranging from timing misalignment to redundant workflows. Using a prioritization matrix, we created a phased roadmap, focusing first on the changes with the biggest impact.
Wisdom Mining Workshop
Working closely with the Insync team, we mapped the product’s core user flows and structured the sprint around clusters of related features, enabling rapid prototyping while ensuring cohesion.
Working with a team so invested in feedback loops and continuous improvement made this project not just a design exercise, but a genuinely collaborative build. As of 2025, the product is in active development, with future iterations rolling out based on learnings from early users, built on a flexible system we crafted together.

The brand is at the forefront of advancing business capabilities through cutting-edge support automation solutions with AI. With a vision to make Conversational AI more accessible and easy to implement for enterprises, without additional time or effort, the brand empowers businesses to enhance productivity, optimise processes, and unlock new growth opportunities. We worked with the brand to design a product that empowers customers to create custom chatbots, using an intuitive interface that offers both flexibility and efficiency in chatbot deployment. With a focus on user-friendly design, the product enables seamless chatbot configuration and adaptation with reduced development time and costs, while driving higher conversion rates.

FIGMA SECTION: Slide 16:9 - 313
(3 unique strings)
Sample layer paths:
  - Document/Page 1/Slide 16:9 - 313/Text
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Collaborated with
Designing a single, intuitive dashboard helped bridge silos and enabled cross-country visibility. It allowed AGRA’s leadership to track KPIs in real time, spot problem areas faster, and make more coordinated, data-driven decisions.
Insight The success of the dashboard depended less on aesthetics and more on how seamlessly it functioned within AGRA’s existing systems and workflows. Ensuring compatibility with tools like StarRez and meeting operational requirements became central to the design process, proving that usability and integration mattered more than visual polish.

The header consists of search bar for easy access, any notification related to tasks assigned or comments on canvas, and CTA to create a new project.
The user can chat with the ai agent, communicate the strategy and ask for a predefined components or readily made pipeline diagram.
The user can get teams and individuals onboard right from the start of the project and later assign the tasks.
The user can switch between the visual canvas and the underlying code/logic for custom Node manipulation. Options provided are (pipeline coding, python coding, chart, table)
The users can see a summary of the projects, with the final outcome which was published, which is a editable file.
To make models live, an interaction node was introduced that instantly update backtest visualizations.

FIGMA SECTION: Uncategorized
(10 unique strings)
Sample layer paths:
  - Document/Page 1/Text
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Challenges - 1. The first key challenge was to balance various stakeholders and their requirements - each stakeholder even within the organization had a different requirement from the design. Development team - R-shiny compatibilities Within Agra - Leaderships - overall goal tracking with a larger picture view, country leaders - specific country tracking, business lines - Progress tracking of their particular sector within the organization Project manager - An aesthetic looking dashboard that looks attractive and using multiple types of visualizations The project was done in collaboration with the development team in the United states, the actual organisation stakeholders in Africa, and the design team in India, spanning different geographies, languages, and time zones requiring a lot of coordination. The limitations of the development software which caused design sacrifices
Collaborated with
Designing a single, intuitive dashboard helped bridge silos and enabled cross-country visibility. It allowed AGRA’s leadership to track KPIs in real time, spot problem areas faster, and make more coordinated, data-driven decisions.
Hand off Making sure that the designs were compatible with R-shiny a template based development tool was key which was going to be used by the organization, so each layout, interaction and feature was developed keeping that in mind. Designing for each use case scenario so that situations like no data, zero states, activated states of each feature and everything else was designed and incorporated in the designs Creating design for scale was important to define for the future, so that even with the absence of a designer, future growth of the platform could take place through modular design layouts and developing design systems.
Insight The success of the dashboard depended less on aesthetics and more on how seamlessly it functioned within AGRA’s existing systems and workflows. Ensuring compatibility with tools like StarRez and meeting operational requirements became central to the design process, proving that usability and integration mattered more than visual polish.
Interviews revealed that decision-makers weren’t struggling with lack of information but with overwhelming, unstructured data. They needed a concise, visual way to compare performance across countries and metrics — something that turned data into direction.
Learnings and reflection 1. Functionality outweighs aesthetics. Working with AGRA reinforced that visual appeal means little without strong functionality and integration. Ensuring the dashboard aligned with systems like StarRez and supported real user workflows mattered far more than surface-level polish. 2. Designing for clarity means designing for decisions. The project showed how effective design isn’t just about displaying data—it’s about helping people act on it. Turning complex, fragmented datasets into clear, comparable visuals taught you the importance of structuring information to drive confident, data-backed decisions. 3. Collaboration sharpens design intent. Partnering with diverse stakeholders across technical and leadership teams highlighted how collaboration shapes better outcomes. Listening to varying needs helped balance strategic goals with on-ground usability, strengthening your approach to human-centered design.
The solution turned a complex data challenge into a visually engaging, user-friendly tool. A high-level summary on the landing page highlighted key goals for instant comprehension, while micro-visualizations offered snapshot insights with hover or click details. The final choices ensured consistency in color, navigation, and visual hierarchy for a seamless experience. Each visualization was assessed for clarity, comparisons across regions and time periods, and quick trend detection, using tailored approaches like grouped bar and line graphs.
To design a tool that allows garlenders to manufacture and make garlands faster and quicker to produce more units and meet the requirments of the consumers across various varities.
Tradeoffs Clarity vs visual appeal Some visualizations were visually engaging but difficult for leadership teams to interpret quickly. Simplicity was prioritized over visual complexity. Scalability vs detail Certain graph types worked well initially but became harder to maintain and compare as the dataset grew. Consistency vs experimentation The introduction of micro visualizations created a visual disconnect from the more detailed charts, requiring greater consistency across the dashboard.
